ü¶Ä Um, I said ‚Äúhi‚Äù.

ü¶Ä Wait!

ü¶Ä It literally never ends, every time you call .next() it just clones a new value

ü¶Ä Ok enough of that.

ü¶Ä We can also repeat existing iterators infinitely using a method on the iterator called .cycle()

ü¶Ä Once the iterator has run out of items, instead of returning a None, it simply starts again.

You can also create iterators by combining other iterators with .chain(), although they have to be of the same type:

Many other Types in Rust can also be broken down into Iterators.

The script for this video, for example, can be represented as one large str, which you can break the data down by .lines(), .chars() or .bytes() all of which produce iterators.

Cool ways to use Iterators

Mathematics

One common thing we might want to do is consume an iterator of numeric values and get some new value from it.

A quick warning though!

Unlike some other operations we might perform on iterators which add some step that will be performed when we call .next, methods that ‚Äúconsume‚Äù the Iterator will actually try to process everything in the Iterator.

This means if you call them on an infinite iterator, like those created by .repeat() or .cycle(), your code will enter an infinite loop and never end.

However, when that‚Äôs not the case there‚Äôs some useful mechanisms we can use:

ü¶Ä For iterators of items that implement the Sum trait (for example numbers) the .sum() method will add all the items in the iterator together:

ü¶Ä You‚Äôll notice we have the turbofish operator again as we need to know what type to sum to

ü¶Ä For iterators of items that implement the Product trait (eg, again, numbers) the .product() method will multiply all the items in the iterator together:

ü¶Ä Its worth noting that some surprising things implement Sum and Product, including blanket implementations for Option<T> and Result<T, E> where T already implements the trait.

ü¶Ä When doing it this way, for some reason, the Option needs to be owned, so here we‚Äôve .into_iter()‚Äôd the collection, as we don‚Äôt need to use the collection afterward.

ü¶Ä Or, if we do need to keep the collection, because i32 is Copy, we could chain .iter() with .copied()

ü¶Ä I want to add another quick warning here, .sum() and .product() use the basic add and multiply operators respectively.

ü¶Ä This can be problematic because there‚Äôs no check to see if the result still fits inside the numeric type.

ü¶Ä For example, this is fine

// FIXME

ü¶Ä But this will panic

ü¶Ä But you won‚Äôt know that until runtime.

ü¶Ä Usually this won‚Äôt be a problem as you‚Äôll likely be using number types with a lot of space, not u8s, but we‚Äôll touch on slower (but safer) ways to get around this later.

ü¶Ä Anyway, carrying on:

ü¶Ä For iterators of items that implement Ord you can use .min() and .max() to find the smallest and largest values respectively.

ü¶Ä Chars are ordered by their numeric value so capitals come first, making ‚ÄúH‚Äù the smallest value and ‚Äúw‚Äù the ‚Äúlargest‚Äù value

ü¶Ä If you just want to know how many items there are, you can use .count() which merely tells us how many items are in an iterator.

ü¶Ä However, count consumes iterator, meaning you can‚Äôt use the iterator after using count

ü¶Ä If the iterator implements ExactSizeIterator though, and many iterators do, then you can use .len() instead.

ü¶Ä Not only does this not consume the iterator, it‚Äôs almost always faster to get the same result:

Applying a Process over each item

One of the most common uses for Iterators is to process a set of Items one at a time.

There are a number of methods on the Iterator trait (that themselves return new Iterators) that are really helpful for this.

In fact, it‚Äôs common to chain multiple iterators together in this way.

Let‚Äôs start with one of the simplest.

You can take an iterator and exclude Items based on the result of a predicate using .filter().

ü¶Ä For example, we could take a range of numbers, and filter out all odd numbers like this:

ü¶Ä If we were to look at the count of the iterators before and after the filter, you‚Äôll see they‚Äôve changed!

ü¶Ä Now, I was going to point out that ideally we should use .len() but that .filter() can not return an ExactSizeIterator.

ü¶Ä But, to my surprise, ExactSizeIterator isn‚Äôt implemented for RangeInclusive either‚Ä¶ but it is for Range.

ü¶Ä So, here‚Äôs a quick edit to the code that does the same thing.

ü¶Ä Now we can use .len() on the range, much better

ü¶Ä I don‚Äôt know why RangeInclusive isn‚Äôt ExactSizeIterator, if you do, let me know in the comments, but I think it‚Äôs a sign to prefer Range over RangeInclusive.

ü¶Ä Anyway, for the obvious reasons we don‚Äôt know if an Item is included or not before an item is processed by the filter, so we don‚Äôt get an ExactSizeIterator back from the .filter() method, so we have to count each item.

Another great way to process Iterators one Item at a time is to take that Item and transform it in some way.

We can pass a function into the .map() method that receives the item and returns a new value.

If that value is of a different type, the Iterator you get back will also be of that new type:

ü¶Ä If we start off with a Range like this, this is an Iterator where the Item is of type i32.

ü¶Ä We can then use .map() to take the number and return the result of the format macro.

ü¶Ä And now we have an Iterator where the Item is of type String.

ü¶Ä Sometimes the process you apply to an item might itself result in an Option, and rather than having an iterator of Options you may want to discard Nones and unwrap Oks, this is where .filter_map() is really handy.

ü¶Ä This range gives us every valid u8 number in sequence from smallest, zero, to largest, two-five-five

ü¶Ä (Despite what we just discussed this time it has to be RangeInclusive).

ü¶Ä If we add 250 to each number, most of them will no longer fit inside a u8.

ü¶Ä The .checked_add() method found on most numeric types in Rust will return an Option, where the None variant represents an overflow.

ü¶Ä If we combine this with a .filter_map() all numbers that overflow will automatically be excluded (because of the None) and all numbers in the Some variant are automatically unwrapped

ü¶Ä That leaves us with these six numbers.

ü¶Ä This not only saves us from having to deal with doubly wrapped options from next (for example Some(Some(255))) but entirely removes the items from the iterator meaning anything else we chain doesn‚Äôt have to deal with it either.

ü¶Ä By comparison, if we just used a map, we‚Äôd have 256 Options, 250 of which are None.

ü¶Ä Another way to reduce how many items we want to deal with in an iterator is by using .take(n) and .skip(n).

ü¶Ä We can end an iterator earlier by only taking a certain number of items from it with .take(n)

ü¶Ä Or we can skip over a number of items with .skip(n) before resuming the iterator from that point.

ü¶Ä .take() can be particularly useful when working with infinite iterators

ü¶Ä I want you to consider the output of the following program.

ü¶Ä Consider it!

ü¶Ä cough cough

ü¶Ä An Iterator method we used earlier, .enumerate(), allows us to add an index to our Iterator by changing the type of the Item T to a tuple of (usize, T).

ü¶Ä This can be really handy in combination with other iterators when the position in an iterator is important.

ü¶Ä Let‚Äôs say we want to filter every other item out of a Vec.

ü¶Ä We can do that by chaining together several of the Iterators we‚Äôve just learned.

ü¶Ä We‚Äôll take ownership of the data because we don‚Äôt need the original Vector after this.

ü¶Ä We‚Äôll enumerate the iterator so we get the index from the point we‚Äôre at in the iterator onwards (here we‚Äôre at the start, but that might not always be the case)

ü¶Ä The index starts at zero, so by checking the modulo of the index, we can take every other item starting with the first.

ü¶Ä Filter doesn‚Äôt change the data only decides if it should be kept or not, this means we can ignore the original data inside the predicate

ü¶Ä Since we no longer need the index after this, we can map the tuple and return just the data we care about

ü¶Ä Finally, we‚Äôll collect it and test the result

ü¶Ä Any time you see a filter and a map next to each other though, you might be able to abbreviate your code with filter_map.

ü¶Ä Booleans can be turned into Options with .then_some(), so this works, but‚Ä¶

ü¶Ä in my opinion, you should always go with the code that‚Äôs easiest to read, and its up to you to decide what that is

Finally, there are three more consuming methods I want to cover for processing data

.fold() and .reduce() consume iterators and return a single value by modifying that value for each item in the Iterator.

.fold() lets you specify the initial value for the returned value, but .reduce() uses the first item in the iterator as the initial value and continues processing from the next item.

Earlier I mentioned some risk with .sum() and .product() and promised a slower but safer way to do the same thing.

ü¶Ä So let‚Äôs try doing that with .fold()

ü¶Ä it takes two parameters, the first being the initial value, and the second being a closure with two parameters

ü¶Ä We‚Äôll use an Option with a 0 for the initial value, which is why we can‚Äôt use .reduce() in this specific case.

ü¶Ä For the closure, I usually stick to calling the parameters acc and cur representing the ‚Äúacc-umulated‚Äù value which starts as our initial value, and the ‚Äúcur-rent‚Äù value, which is the current Item in the iterator.

ü¶Ä This closure is called for every Item in the iterator and returns the next accumulated value.

ü¶Ä We‚Äôre simply going to add the values together, our accumulated value is an Option, and we only need to add if it‚Äôs a Some variant

ü¶Ä We can use .and_then() to get inside the Option, and we‚Äôll use the same checked_add to increase the value

ü¶Ä This maps our Option to another Option that comes out of checked_add so we don‚Äôt need to do anything else before returning from the closure

ü¶Ä That said, there‚Äôs actually a better way to provide this functionality.

ü¶Ä The way we‚Äôve built this, once we hit our first None, we know the answer is going to be None too, yet we keep processes items

ü¶Ä There‚Äôs a method designed exactly for this, .try_fold()

ü¶Ä Not only will stop iterating on it‚Äôs first None, potentially ending very early, but because it knows we‚Äôre dealing with Options will automatically unwrap our accumulated option for us, making the code much simpler!

ü¶Ä The last consumer method I wanted to talk about is .for_each().

ü¶Ä It lets you do something with each item in the iterator without returning anything.

ü¶Ä The simplest example might be, if we went back to our Fibonacci sequence instead of printing the value in a loop, we can use .for_each() to print the value.

ü¶Ä The lack of return value might feel like it rather limits the usefulness of this method.

ü¶Ä However, it can be useful when doing things like sending data somewhere else, for example, across threads, which we‚Äôll be looking at in the next video.

More Iterator Traits

There are a few more traits you may want to be aware of when making your own iterators, or consuming others.

IntoIterator can be implemented on any type that can be turned into. an‚Ä¶ Iterator.

Ah, I see what they did there.

One place you may find yourself using it is on newtypes, or types where the most important data inside is represented as some kind of collection.

ü¶Ä Let‚Äôs say we have a newtype ‚ÄúAlbums‚Äù, that contains a Vector of ‚ÄúAlbum‚Äù

ü¶Ä In our domain logic, it might make sense that we can start a new collection of Albums and add an Album to it by buying it.

ü¶Ä So we can build an Albums struct like this

ü¶Ä If we were to implement IntoIterator for Albums there are two important associated types that we need to specify.

ü¶Ä Item is easy, that‚Äôll be our Album type.

ü¶Ä IntoIter is the name of the type that is the Iterator.

ü¶Ä Remember back at the start, we created a Fibonacci Iterator.

ü¶Ä Our struct Fibonacci is the Iterator type.

ü¶Ä What the trait is actually asking us for here is the data type that will manage the iteration process for us.

ü¶Ä We‚Äôre using a Vec internally, and Vec has a generic Iterator struct it uses when you turn a Vec into an Iterator, so we can use that, filling in the Generic part with our Item type.

ü¶Ä All we need to do in our case then, is return the result of calling Vec .into_iter()

ü¶Ä Once we‚Äôve done that, turning our Albums type into an Iterator of Album is trivial.

ü¶Ä This code turns our Albums collection into a list of Artists

ü¶Ä But what if we want to go back to having our Albums type again.

ü¶Ä FromIterator allows you to turn an Iterator into another type, usually through the .collect() method on an Iterator

ü¶Ä In our case, again, we could really just lean into the existing Vec type‚Äôs utilities,

ü¶Ä But you could also rely on the structs own implementation to do the same thing by looping through the iterator.

ü¶Ä Now we can create our Albums struct from any Iterator of Album

Finally, the last two traits you should be aware of are DoubleEndedIterator and ExactSizeIterator.

We could implement these for our Album type, but we‚Äôd really just be wrapping Vec, so I‚Äôll give some more direct examples.

ü¶Ä We‚Äôve spoken about ExactSizeIterator, it can tell you the size of the iterator without consuming it, using the .len() method

ü¶Ä DoubleEndedIterator allows you to reverse the order of an Iterator with .rev().

ü¶Ä The Iterators returned from all collections in the standard library are all, both of these

ü¶Ä To my surprise, even the Iter structs used for LinkedList and BinaryHeap are both DoubleEndedIterator, I didn‚Äôt expect that.

Threads

Threads allow us to build programs where parts of the program can run independently of one another.

Threads can (big emphasis on ‚Äúcan‚Äù) help you make faster and more responsive programs.

For example:

  • As a web developer, I would like the server framework I‚Äôm using to start responding to the next request before it‚Äôs finished responding to the previous request
  • As a game developer, I would like my game engine to capture player inputs without being blocked by the renderer
  • As a data engineer, I would like to process large sets of data with parallelism

This series is accompanied by a free book, check the description for a link straight to this chapter.

My name is Daniel, welcome to IRISS.

Intro

We‚Äôre going to step through:

  1. how we can run code in a thread, including sending data before it starts
  2. how we can wait for a thread to end, including receiving data when it ends
  3. how we can communicate with running threads
  4. and how we can share state between threads

We‚Äôll also be touching again on our marker traits Send and Sync

What is a thread?

Before we get into the Rust, it‚Äôs worth discussing what a thread actually is.

When you run a program, that specific instance of the program is called a Process.

The process incorporates not just the instructions to be run but is an abstraction around various resources that the program has access to, such as memory.

You can run multiple processes which the operating system will schedule separately which could allow you to do more things at once, however, those processes won‚Äôt (or at least, shouldn‚Äôt) have access to the same memory.

There are ways to communicate between processes, but they can be slower and more restrictive than if we could share memory.

The specific part of the Process responsible for executing your code is called a thread, and a single process will always have at least one thread but can have many.

Threads are scheduled by the operating system independently, allowing one process to do multiple things effectively concurrently.

Starting a thread

ü¶Äüë®üèª Your program always has at least one thread, even your basic hello-world program runs in a thread.

ü¶Äüë®üèª What we‚Äôre interested in today is how we start more threads.

ü¶Äüë®üèª This is a process called Spawning.

ü¶Äüë®üèª To spawn a thread, we use std::thread::spawn‚Ä¶ but, this will do little on its own.

ü¶Äüë®üèª If we run this code, can you see what‚Äôs missing in the output?

ü¶Äüë®üèª It looks like our thread didn‚Äôt run‚Ä¶ but actually, its even worse than that.

ü¶Äüë®üèª When writing this portion of the book, this is the output I consistently got from my Mac, and from Rust Playground.

ü¶Äüë®üèª That consistency actually surprised me a little, but then when writing the script for this video on my Windows machine‚Ä¶

ü¶Äüë®üèª I got this.

ü¶Äüë®üèª This is the inconsistency I was actually expecting, its not that our thread doesn‚Äôt run, it‚Äôs that it might‚Ä¶ or it might not.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Spawning a thread returns a JoinHandle.

ü¶Äüë®üèª The JoinHandle is what ties the spawned thread to the thread that spawned it.

ü¶Äüë®üèª When the JoinHandle is dropped, which in this case happens instantly, the thread is orphaned.

ü¶Äüë®üèª It may still run but, in this case, the process ends at the end of main, so whether the child thread runs or not, is no longer up to us.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We can tell our main thread to pause and wait for a running thread to end by calling join on the JoinHandle.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We‚Äôve been using closures, but functions work just as well and can be better for more complex programs.

üìï The only restrictions are:

üìï - the function needs to be ‚ÄúFnOnce returning T, Send and static‚Äù

üìï - and T also needs to be ‚ÄúSend and static‚Äù

Exactly when threads are allowed to execute code is controlled by a scheduler which we can‚Äôt directly manage ourselves, but we can influence it.

Putting one thread to sleep can allow another thread to run.

ü¶Ä If we run this code as-is, you‚Äôll see the main thread runs to completion before the child thread starts

ü¶Ä If we put each thread to sleep after it writes to standard out, we can ‚Äúencourage‚Äù the scheduler to start work on the other thread if it isn‚Äôt already.

ü¶Äüë®üèª There‚Äôs two very important things I want you to note here though

ü¶Äüë®üèª Firstly, thread::sleep is not a timer, your thread may sleep for longer than you requested or not at all in certain situations.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Secondly, the scheduler ultimately decides when code is run, so there‚Äôs no guarantee you will get this exact output, so bare that in mind if when code runs matters to you.

So now we can run threads, let‚Äôs start looking at how to send data back and forth between them.

We can pass data into a thread before it starts so long as the data is Send.

We previously talked about this trait in the Traits video, but to recap, data is Send so long as it can be safely sent between threads.

This trait is automatically implemented for all types that can be Send (though it is also possible to opt out of it).

ü¶Äüë®üèª We can move data into the closure that will be sent to the thread using the move keyword.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Here we move the entire data vector into the spawned thread.

ü¶Äüë®üèª You can also return data via the join handler.

ü¶Äüë®üèª This means you could pass hard work to a thread and do other work, coming back to check on the thread at a later time.

ü¶Äüë®üèª A little side note, if it‚Äôs helpful, you can check if the thread is finished with .is_finished().

ü¶Äüë®üèª Here I‚Äôm using it to let the person running the program know we‚Äôre still waiting on the thread to do its work, but the program is still running.

Sending messages

Now we can start one thread, there‚Äôs no stopping us!

Modern schedulers can manage a lot of threads at once, however, so far, we can only send data between a child thread and the parent that started it before and after that thread runs, not during.

What if we want to communicate across multiple threads, or send data to a thread while its running?

üìï Multi-producer, single-consumer (MPSC) is a queue pattern that allows us to create channels with multiple Senders that can send messages, and a single Reciever that can receive them.

üìï As per the name, Multi-producer, you can clone Senders but each of those clones can only send to a single Reciever.

The Sender and Receiver types are Send meaning that you can create them in one thread and send them to another.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Let‚Äôs create a bunch of threads and give each of them a Sender that points back to a single Reciever

ü¶Äüë®üèª We move the sender into the closure being run in the map method of an iterator.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Because the closure now owns it, we can clone it for each thread we‚Äôre spawning

ü¶Äüë®üèª And move the cloned sender to the child thread.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We‚Äôll send the Reciever to a final thread that will collect the data from the other threads.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Finally, we‚Äôll join all the threads with a sender, before we join the thread with the receiver.

ü¶Äüë®üèª This is important because this is the first bit of code we‚Äôve produced that can cause a deadlock, where two or more threads get blocked by each other

ü¶Äüë®üèª If you forget to join the senders first, they won‚Äôt send their messages but will block the receiver thread here, so your program gets stuck.

For what its worth, there‚Äôs no built-in way to create a channel with multiple receivers (Receiver is not Clone), however, there‚Äôs nothing stopping you building your own type for that, or there are crates that support it like Crossbeam.

Sharing State

So now we can send messages across threads, but what if we need multiple threads to have access to the same data, maybe even able to edit that data.

To do this, we need to use types that implement the Sync trait.

Something is Send if it can be sent between threads, but doing this moves ownership from one thread to another.

Something is Sync if a reference to it can be sent between threads

i.e. T is Sync if a reference T is Send.

Most things are Sync, but we still have to abide the rules of references in that we can have as many immutable references to something as we like, but we can only have one mutable reference.

Furthermore, references cannot outlive the data they reference‚Ä¶ which is a little harder to confirm with threads.

How do you know the thread referencing your data doesn‚Äôt exist for longer than the data it‚Äôs referenced?

One option is using scoped threads which we can create with std::thread::scope.

ü¶Äüë®üèª The scope function takes a closure with a single parameter that gives us the scope context.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We can use the scope context to spawn threads.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We don‚Äôt need to keep track of JoinHandles this time, all scoped threads are joined at the end of the scope closure.

ü¶Äüë®üèª However, the spawn method on the scope context does still return a JoinHandle if you want to handle potential thread panics.

ü¶Äüë®üèª The scope function blocks the thread that called it, until all the threads it started have been joined, so after that we‚Äôre safe to modify the data again, which here would require a mutable reference.

ü¶Äüë®üèª You can also send mutable references across threads like this, but, bear in mind, only one thread can access the mutable reference, and it must end before we can access our data again.

use std::thread::scope;

fn main() {
    let mut data = String::from("This is owned by the main thread");

    scope(|s| {
        s.spawn(|| {
            data.push_str(" but can be modified by a child thread");
        });
    });

    assert_eq!(
        &data,
        "This is owned by the main thread but can be modified by a child thread"
    );
}

So we can share readable data across multiple threads with immutable references, or share writable data temporarily with a single thread, but what if we want to share read/write access to data across multiple threads.

Let‚Äôs start by thinking why we can‚Äôt do this with just references.

When we‚Äôre using threads, multiple parts of our program can be executed at the same time.

Imagine we have two threads that want to change the data, behind a reference, based on what is currently stored there, something simple like each thread wants to multiply a numeric value.

  1. Thread 1 reads the value from memory into a register
  2. Thread 2 reads the value from memory into a register
  3. Thread 1 multiplies the data and stores it back in memory
  4. Thread 2 multiplies the data and stores it back in memory

In this situation, we‚Äôve lost the effect of Thread 1, which could be a bug.

Let‚Äôs consider a more serious version of this.

Imagine the data rather than just being a single value, is an image stored in an array like structure, and you‚Äôre applying multiple processes to the image at the same time.

This time, if one thread were to override another‚Äôs work, we have a much more obvious problem.

To get around this, we need to prevent two threads accessing the same piece of data at the same time.

There is a general software abstraction concept called a ‚Äúmutex‚Äù that makes access to data MUTually EXclusive.

Rust provides it‚Äôs mutex through std::sync::Mutex.

Once you place data inside a Mutex, to access it again, you need to ‚Äúlock‚Äù the Mutex.

ü¶Äüë®üèª If the Mutex is already locked, then the thread currently trying to access the data needs to wait for the existing lock to be released.

ü¶Äüë®üèª the lock method on a Mutex returns a MutexGuard

ü¶Äüë®üèª When the MutexGuard goes out of scope, the lock is dropped, allowing other threads to use the Mutex

ü¶Äüë®üèª If a thread panics while holding a MutexGuard, this poisons the Mutex, so locking a Mutex returns a result.

However, there‚Äôs still a slight problem here.

We‚Äôre currently very dependent on using scoped threads because we need our references to point back to the owned data, but scoped threads aren‚Äôt the norm.

In fact, most of the time you use threads in Rust, they will probably be abstracted behind some other framework (for example, a web server, a game engine, or data processing tools).

The problem, of course, is that we don‚Äôt know when the owned data will go out of scope and no longer be accessible.

We can solve this problem using an Atomic Reference Count.

We haven‚Äôt discussed reference counting yet as it‚Äôs usually fairly niche, however, reference counting allows you to share data around an application without needing to clone it and side stepping complex reference rules or lifetimes.

It works by moving the data you want to share onto the heap, and allowing access through a reference count type.

When you clone the reference count value, instead of the data being cloned, it modifies its internal count of how many clones currently exist.

Every time a reference count type goes out of scope, the count is decreased.

Once the count hits zero, there are no further references to the data and so it can be safely dropped.

Now, if you‚Äôve paid attention as to why we need a Mutex for modifying data across threads, you‚Äôll see that using a normal reference count won‚Äôt work.

If the reference counter is cloned or dropped while also being cloned or dropped in another thread, you could end up with an inconsistent count of references, meaning data gets dropped at the wrong time.

This is why we need a special reference count type, std::sync::Arc, an Atomic Reference Count.

Atomic data types guarantee atomic changes.

Atomic changes are guaranteed to appear to be instantaneous to all external observers, meaning that two threads can change the value, but that these changes cannot overlap.

Arc is a little slower than Rusts built in basic reference counting type std::rc::Rc, but prevents corruption across threads.

Editors note: I don‚Äôt think I‚Äôve ever used Rc, but I use Arc all the time, so don‚Äôt worry that we didn‚Äôt cover it in this series.

If you need to pass data around a single thread, wrapped in its own container, it‚Äôs there for you to use

ü¶Äüë®üèª So, armed with this knowledge, we can go back to unscoped threads!

ü¶Äüë®üèª Like earlier, we can clone the arc in the map method of the iterator, and move the cloned value to the thread.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Arc also implements Deref for its internal type, so we can call lock on the Mutex from the Arc.

And that‚Äôs it!

Hopefully threads feel a lot more accessible now, but let me know in the comments if you have any questions or want to share tips, tricks, or things I missed with others.

Macros

Macro‚Äôs let us do meta-programming in Rust.

Metaprogramming allows us to treat our code as data; manipulate it, expand it, and generate new code.

Macro‚Äôs let us do a lot of things, but I‚Äôve broken this video into three parts

  1. We‚Äôll go over the basics of how you can use macro_rules! to make your own macros

  2. I‚Äôll show a somewhat real life use case I‚Äôve been working with lately

  3. And we‚Äôll implement another programming language within Rust to demonstrate how you can create domain-specific languages (DSLs)

This series is accompanied by a free book, check the description for a link straight to this chapter.

My name is Daniel, welcome to IRISS.

There are two types of macro in Rust, macro_rules!, also known as declarative macros, or macros by example‚Ä¶ and proc macros.

We won‚Äôt be dealing with proc macros in this series, but they allow you to create custom Derive macros and custom attributes.

They also let you make the same function style macros we‚Äôll be making with macro_rules! but can unlock even more power!

If you‚Äôd like me to do a video on this after the IRISS series, let me know in the comments.

Today though, we‚Äôre just covering macro_rules!

Anatomy of macro_rules!

macro_rules! is, itself, a macro, providing its own domain specific language that allows you to create more macros.

This gets very powerful and, honestly, very weird.

Let‚Äôs take it slow.

ü¶Äüë®üèª The general layout of macro_rules! looks like this:

ü¶Äüë®üèª We write macro_rules!, with an exclamation mark, followed by the name the macro we‚Äôre creating.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We then have a block containing a list of rules.

ü¶Äüë®üèª The rule contains a pattern to match on, which itself potentially contains metavariables which we‚Äôll discuss later.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Each rule also has a block that describes how code will be generated when the macro is invoked with a matching pattern.

Rather than it generating code with a simple copy/paste, macros work on the Abstract Syntax Tree or AST.

This is an intermediate step of the compilation process where your code has already been turned into datastructures that represents what your program does.

This makes it much safer and more fully featured that copy-paste style macros you might have worked with in the past.

Hello, macro!

We‚Äôll start by making a hello world macro that produces a String.

ü¶Äüë®üèª As we said a moment ago, immediately after macro_rules! we provide the name of the macro we‚Äôre creating, in this case hello.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Our first draft won‚Äôt match anything between the brackets, so we leave those empty.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We then have an arrow, followed by some curly brackets surrounding what our macro will generate.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Here‚Äôs a little test for it to show how it might be used.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Our hello macro simply creates a string containing "Hello, world" at the site where the macro is called (in this case inside an assert_eq! macro).

ü¶Äüë®üèª This type of macro could be useful if you have a block of code you need to repeat but don‚Äôt want to put in a function.

ü¶Äüë®üèª But let‚Äôs be honest, that‚Äôs not going to come up very often.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Let‚Äôs upgrade our macro to match a pattern.

ü¶Äüë®üèª What madness is this?!

ü¶Äüë®üèª What kind of parameters are we passing to this macro?

ü¶Äüë®üèª The key to understanding the power of macros is that they don‚Äôt take parameters.

ü¶Äüë®üèª The thing in the brackets at the start of each rule is a pattern, and that pattern can be almost anything.

ü¶Äüë®üèª The content of the macro‚Äôs invocation is broken up into something called a token tree, which we‚Äôll talk about in the next section.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Here, this must be present is parsed as four token trees: this, must, be and present.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Different tokens won‚Äôt match our pattern, so this won‚Äôt compile.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We can invoke different rules based on the matched pattern.

ü¶Äüë®üèª So we could match several people by making a rule for each of them.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We obviously can‚Äôt write out every possible thing that we might want match on though.

ü¶Äüë®üèª What if we want to be able to say ‚Äúhello‚Äù to lots of different people?

ü¶Äüë®üèª Instead, we can capture tokens into metavariables.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Things got a little bit weird here, right?

ü¶Äüë®üèª Let‚Äôs step through our changes.

ü¶Äüë®üèª First, we added a metavariable, and you‚Äôll immediately notice this looks nothing like a normal function parameter in Rust.

ü¶Äüë®üèª In macro_rules!, we can parameterise tokens into ‚Äúmetavariables‚Äù which are preceded by a dollar symbol, followed by a colon, and what‚Äôs called a fragment-specifier.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Fragment-specifiers are a bit like types but are specific to how Rust classifies tokens trees.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We can‚Äôt specify str here, but we can specify that we expect a literal, which is any raw value, such as a string slice, a number, a boolean, etc.

ü¶Äüë®üèª You might still wonder what happens if our macro gets a literal that‚Äôs not a str and the answer is it won‚Äôt compile.

ü¶Äüë®üèª The person who passed in the non-str will get an error relating to the .push_str method on String though admittedly errors on macros like this can be a little harder to work with.

ü¶Äüë®üèª That said, over the 10 years since Rust came out, contributors to the language have done a lot of work to clarify errors across the board.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Anyway, there are a number of different fragment-specifiers, some of which overlap with each other, we‚Äôll go over more of them in the next section.

ü¶Äüë®üèª The second change we‚Äôve made here is that inside of the code block‚Ä¶ we‚Äôve added another block.

ü¶Äüë®üèª The reason for this is that when we invoke the macro, Rust generates code at the point that you place the macro.

ü¶Äüë®üèª If we didn‚Äôt have the extra brackets, when we use the macro in our assert_eq!, our code would look to Rust as if it were this:

ü¶Äüë®üèª This doesn‚Äôt work because assert_eq!, which is also a macro, expects to match expressions, represented by the fragment-specifier :expr.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Oh‚Ä¶ I‚Äôm going to have to work out how to vocalise a few unique things this episode, wish me luck!

In Rust, an expression is a segment of code that produces a value.

So String::from("Hello, ") is an expression, but let mut output = String::from("Hello, "); is not, that‚Äôs a :stmt, a statement.

Blocks of code, even multiple statements, surrounded by curly brackets are expressions though, they have a value, even if the value is the unit type.

When we wrap our macro in curly brackets then, and have some value as the final line, our code block becomes a single expression the value and type of which matches that final value.

ü¶Äüë®üèª This means that when we add those extra curly brackets to our macro, the generated code now looks like this, which is valid!

ü¶Äüë®üèª Expressions in Rust are particularly useful as they have a type and a value, just like variables, allowing you to use them inside other expressions.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Let‚Äôs go deeper and add another rule.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Let‚Äôs bring back our original behaviour for an empty hello! macro:

ü¶Äüë®üèª This is fine, but we‚Äôre repeating ourselves a little bit.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We should avoid having two copies of the "Hello, " string slice.

ü¶Äüë®üèª To maintain consistency, we can call our macro recursively!

We‚Äôre nearly there now, but I think our hello macro is missing one critical feature; what if we want to greet lots of people at the same time?

We can ‚Äúrepeat‚Äù patterns inside macros by surrounding them with parenthesis preceded by a dollar and followed by either a ?, a +, or a *.

Similar to regex rules:

  • ? means the content is repeated zero or one times
  • + means one or more times
  • and * means zero or more times

You can add a separator to the repeat pattern by placing it before the repeat character.

This token can be almost anything except the repeat tokens or delimiter tokens.

The most common separators you‚Äôre likely to use are commas or semicolons, but you could use something like tilde‚Äôs‚Ä¶ ya know‚Ä¶ if you‚Äôre twisted.

Repeats are used in rule matchers to match patterns, including metavariables, multiple times

They‚Äôre also used in code generation to repeat code for each repeated metavariable.

We already have zero and one metavariable dealt with, so we want a rule in our macro that takes two or more inputs

ü¶Äüë®üèª For want of space, I‚Äôm going to condense the formatting from now on, so keep an eye out for those double curly brackets

ü¶Äüë®üèª Our new rule looks a bit like the previous one, but now there‚Äôs a comma after $name:literal and then a repeat pattern.

ü¶Äüë®üèª The repeat pattern contains a metavariable, $rest:literal, which will be used to store all metavariables passed to the macro after the first.

ü¶Äüë®üèª It uses a + to show that there must be at least one additional metavariable, but there may be many.

ü¶Äüë®üèª In the body of the macro, we initialise our output in much the same way as we do in the version with no inputs, by calling the hello macro with the first metavariable.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We then have another repeat pattern that contains code with the $rest metavariable.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Because we have a repeated metavariable inside a repeated block, this block will be repeated for every literal that $rest matched to.

ü¶Äüë®üèª If we were to unwrap the code generated for the final test, it would look something like this:

Hopefully, you‚Äôre starting to see why writing a quick macro can really cut down on repeated boilerplate code, and we‚Äôre really only making a quick toy macro to demonstrate the power they provide!

You might be wondering if we can use repeats to reduce the number of match arms we have.

We unfortunately can‚Äôt do things like treat the first or last element of a repeat differently using macro repeats undecided look,

but we can merge the second and third arms using a *.

ü¶Äüë®üèª You‚Äôll notice that the , after $name:literal has moved inside the repeat pattern, and the , being used as a separator for the repeat has been dropped.

ü¶Äüë®üèª This is because if we were to try to match ($name:literal, $($rest:literal),*) then we‚Äôd have to use the comma after the first literal so hello!("Yuki") would have to be hello!("Yuki", ) to work.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Instead, we‚Äôve moved the comma token to the beginning of the repeat pattern which can contain things that aren‚Äôt metavariables too.

Ok, so I wasn‚Äôt exactly lying about not being able to treat the first and last elements differently with macro repeats‚Ä¶ we can‚Äôt do it with just macro repeats

BUT, we can work around that with very low-cost language features like slices.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We can split the names out directly into an array.

ü¶Äüë®üèª This will be part of the binary, created at compile time, so doesn‚Äôt require any heap allocations

ü¶Äüë®üèª Next let‚Äôs get an iterator over the array.

ü¶Äüë®üèª By precisely specifying the type of the iterator here we can avoid Rust not knowing what type to infer if the iterator is empty.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We‚Äôll initialise our string as before.

ü¶Äüë®üèª If no metavariables were passed, then the array will be empty, so we‚Äôll use our default value, otherwise we take the first item

ü¶Äüë®üèª Then we‚Äôll loop until no more items are in the iterator

ü¶Äüë®üèª By looking ahead to see if there are more items, we can now use grammatically correct separators between names

ü¶Äüë®üèª And we‚Äôll add an exclamation mark for funsies!

ü¶Äüë®üèª Finally, we do need to update our tests for the improved grammar

‚Äî Missing Image ‚Äî

Being able to quickly compose macros like this can save us a lot of time when repeating the same code over and over.

Tokens, Metavariables, and Fragment-Specifiers

Rust (like most languages) turns your human written code into tokens that it can process.

Groups of tokens can form a token tree.

If tokens are protons and neutrons, then token trees are atoms, and are the smallest thing that we can process in macro_rules!.

An important differentiation with Token Trees to a simple list of tokens are that delimiters (that‚Äôs parentheses, square brackets and curly brackets) are matched up for us.

For example, if we break the statement let hello = String::from("Hello"); into tokens, its looks like this.

But if we break it into token trees, then the parenthesis is a single token tree containing the token tree ‚ÄúHello‚Äù.

In the previous hello! example, we captured tokens that were literals into metavariables with fragment-specifiers, but we can categorise tokens and token trees as more than just literals in macro_rules!.

Here‚Äôs a quick rundown of some of the most common fragment-specifiers:

tt matches a token tree, which is any single token (other than delimiters) or valid collection of delimited tokens.

Remember when we wrote this must be present in our silly example, each word is a token tree.

Token trees can be delimited by parentheses, square brackets or curly brackets so while this must be present is four token trees, we can make it a single token tree containing four token trees by surrounding it in brackets.

literal is the specifier we already used to match against a literal value.

This matches integers, floats, booleans, characters and a whole set of string types including; string literals, raw string literals, byte string literals, C string literals, and so on.

expr it short for ‚Äúexpression‚Äù.

An expression is any token tree that has a value.

For example, String::from("Hello") is an expression, but let hello = String::from("Hello"); is not.

block is specifically a block expression.

Like the code we were generating in our hello! example, blocks can have multiple statements surrounded with curly brackets {...} to make it a block expression.

Of course, all block expressions are also expressions so you may not end up using this fragment specifier too often

stmt is short for ‚Äústatement‚Äù.

This is a line of code or an item.

For example let hello = String::from("Hello"); is a statement but so are items like modules, structs and functions.

ident is short for ‚Äúidentifier‚Äù.

These are things like variable names, type names, or any word that‚Äôs not specifically a keyword.

That said, you can make a raw identifier using r#, so while true is not an identifier because it‚Äôs a keyword, r#true is an identifier.

You‚Äôre most likely to see raw identifiers in macros, particularly ones that are for domain specific languages.

For example, DSLs that are used to create HTML might want to let you use the ‚Äútype‚Äù keyword, as it‚Äôs an HTML attribute, so you might see r#type to make it a raw identifier.

In our earlier this must be present example, each of those tokens is also an identifier, identifiers don‚Äôt need to exist in code.

path is a type path.

This could be an identifier on its own, or a sequence of identifiers seperated by double colon tokens.

Like with identifiers, they don‚Äôt need to exist within the code, they just need to fit the pattern.

ty is short for ‚Äútype‚Äù.

This could be a type or a type description.

For example (dyn Clone + Send) is what‚Äôs called a parenthesised type, though traits like Clone and Send are also types on their own, as are structs like String.

item is anything that could belong to a crate, such as functions, modules, static items, use statements, etc.

vis short for ‚Äúvisibility‚Äù describes the visibility of something else for example pub, pub(crate), or pub(super).

lifetime matches lifetimes such as 'a or 'static

meta, this is a weird one, it matches attributes.

It could be useful if you want to construct a type and pass in attributes to apply to it.

There‚Äôs a lot here, and I‚Äôve ignored the backwards compatible fragment specifiers (some specifiers have changed behaviour over the years).

If you want to see the full list of fragment-specifiers, or more complete descriptions of each of them, check out the official documentation, link in the description

Usefully DRY

The example we ran through earlier to build up our understanding of how macro‚Äôs work was very abstract and not very useful, so I wanted to go over how I‚Äôve started using Macro‚Äôs.

I‚Äôm going to show you how I‚Äôm using macros in my own code, but for the purposes of IRISS I‚Äôve modified the examples to avoid using crates.

Crates are how we share reusable Rust code between each other but, if you haven‚Äôt noticed, I‚Äôve avoided using any in this series to focus purely on the core language.

If you‚Äôre comfortable with crates and async Rust already, and you‚Äôre curious what the differences are in my real code, there‚Äôs a link to the app I‚Äôve been building on stream in the description of this video.

In the Job Tracker app I‚Äôve been building with the help of folks on my streams, I‚Äôve leaned heavily into composing my types using Traits to form common behaviour.

For example, at time of writing, I allow the user to create things like Flags, Roles and Values that belong to Companys, so those types implement the trait HasCompany.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Here‚Äôs an example of how that might look for Role.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Role is pretty simple, it has an id of its own, an id that links it to a company, and a name.

ü¶Äüë®üèªThe HasCompany trait has a single method, get_company_id.

ü¶Äüë®üèª When we implement it for role, it just returns that company_id which is Copy so the code is nice and simple.

ü¶Äüë®üèª I‚Äôm also a huge advocate for unit testing and even for code like this we ought to provide a simple test.

ü¶Äüë®üèª This is pretty nice code, even if I do say so myself.

ü¶Äüë®üèª It‚Äôs clean, easy to understand, easy to use.

ü¶Äüë®üèª But, is it DRY?

DRY in this context, stands for ‚ÄúDon‚Äôt Repeat Yourself‚Äù

Every time we add a new storable item we‚Äôll have to implement the trait and write tests for its implementation.

This isn‚Äôt the only Trait that is applied to ‚Äústorable‚Äù types, and we have multiple ‚Äústorage‚Äù types that also have lots of shared traits that all need implementing and testing.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Let‚Äôs look at how macros can help us generate boilerplate code.

ü¶Äüë®üèª First, we‚Äôll create a macro that will implement HasCompany for any type that has a company_id field.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Obviously if you try to implement this trait on something without this field, the macro will generate code that won‚Äôt compile, but if you need to do that, you can still write your own implementation.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Role does have this field though so we can now implement the trait with one short line

Replacing the test is a little harder though.

We can‚Äôt easily write a generic test that will work for Role and Flag and Values as these types are instantiated in different ways.

What we need to do is have a consistent way to create test instances of the thing being tested.

ü¶Äüë®üèª To do this, I created a TestHelper that can be applied to almost any type, its only job is to return an instantiated value of that type.

ü¶Äüë®üèª This trait exists in a central location

ü¶Äüë®üèª Each test macro sits alongside the trait it creates tests for

ü¶Äüë®üèª We can make this test by taking two metavariables separated by a comma token.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Test name is an ident which we‚Äôll use to name the test function

ü¶Äüë®üèª Storable is what I call any object in this project that can be stored, and in this case is the test subject.

ü¶Äüë®üèª The test here is super rudimentary, we‚Äôre just going to make sure that when we request the id, we get something back.

ü¶Äüë®üèª By implementing the trait for each type that I want to test, I can add tests trivially like this:

ü¶Äüë®üèª This might initially seem like just as much work as before, as we add more traits and more types that implement those traits, the amount of extra work drops dramatically.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Furthermore, there are more complex examples in Fio‚Äôs Job Tracker like the ones that manage the storing and recalling of these storable objects.

Domain Specific Languages

Ever wanted to write your own language?

We‚Äôre going to get a little bit silly shortly‚Ä¶

But Domain Specific Languages (DSLs) can be incredibly useful for conceptualising code in ways meaningful to your domain.

For example, JSX is a DSL for writing React in Javascript.

If you‚Äôre a web developer, ultimately creating HTML, which of these is more readable:

ü¶Äüë®üèª This, which is valid Javascript

const heading = React.createElement(
    'h1',
    {className: 'example'},
    'Hello, world!'
);

ü¶Äüë®üèª Or this, which is not valid JavaScript

const heading = (
    <h1 className="example">
        Hello, world!
    </h1>
);

ü¶Äüë®üèª I think it‚Äôs undeniably easier to understand the one that looks like HTML.

ü¶Äüë®üèª The React DSL will be converted back to JavaScript to be run in a JavaScript runtime.

So, I promised silly, lets write our own DSL‚Ä¶ a Brain Fudge transpiler.

‚Äî pause ‚Äî

A transpiler takes one language and turns it into another, like how Reacts DSL is transpiled into JavaScript

The programming language Brain Fudge was created by Urban M√ºller in 1993.

The language is what‚Äôs known as an ‚Äúesoteric‚Äù language which is, loosely speaking, a fully functional language that you would never actually want to use.

Often they‚Äôre considered jokes, and Brain Fudge certainly is that, but it actually lets us write real programs with just eight instructions.

This makes it ideal for demonstrating how to make a full language inside Rust.

Brain Fudge operates on a theoretically infinite sequence of memory initialised to 0.

You start with a pointer, pointing to the first cell in memory and then process instructions that allow you to:

  • move the pointer
  • modify the data at that point in memory
  • perform loops
  • and either output or input data at the current pointer location.

We do all this with the following eight instructions:

greater than, increments the pointer position, moving it to the next position in memory

less than, decrements the pointer position, moving it to the previous position in memory

add, increments the value at the current position in memory

minus, decrements the value at the current position in memory

period, outputs the value at the current position in memory

comma, takes one word of input and stores it in memory (we won‚Äôt use this in this section though)

square brackets loop the contained code

Each time the loop begins the value at the current position is checked, if the value is 0, it skips the loop.

ü¶Äüë®üèª That sounds easy enough, right‚Ä¶

ü¶Äüë®üèª well, here‚Äôs Hello World in Brain Fudge.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Don‚Äôt panic! We‚Äôre not here to learn Brain Fudge ourselves.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We‚Äôll just trust that this is the Hello World program, we‚Äôll implement the instructions and see what happens when we run it.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We‚Äôre going to use two macros.

ü¶Äüë®üèª First let‚Äôs create a macro that initialises the program.

ü¶Äüë®üèª The input to our transpiler is a repeat pattern of token tree, which we‚Äôll store in the metavariable token.

ü¶Äüë®üèª As it happens the individual characters that make up Brain Fudge are all tokens in Rust so this should work well‚Ä¶ (foreshadowing).

ü¶Äüë®üèª memory is going to be our programs‚Äô memory.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We‚Äôre using a Vec with a single initialised value of 0 under the assumption that even the smallest program requires one word of memory.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Brain Fudge doesn‚Äôt specify a word size, so we‚Äôre going to use 8bits, and we‚Äôll expand the Vec as necessary.

ü¶Äüë®üèª You can use larger words if you like but different programs might function differently depending on what word size is used and how overflows are handled (more on that later).

ü¶Äüë®üèª This is not the most efficient way to do this (we‚Äôll be doing a lot of heap reallocations) but are we taking this seriously?

ü¶Äüë®üèª No, not at all.

ü¶Äüë®üèª pointer points to the current position in memory, or, as we‚Äôll be using it, its the index into our vector

ü¶Äüë®üèª output is where we‚Äôll store output data from the program.

ü¶Äüë®üèª At the end of the macro we take the output Vec of u8s we‚Äôve stored in output and collect it into a string by naively considering each byte to be an ascii character.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Again, depending on how you want to work with your programs, you may want to process the output data differently but this is fine for us.

ü¶Äüë®üèª So now we need to handle the token stream, but before we do that, lets write some tests.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We‚Äôll keep it simple for now, while this is the official Hello World

ü¶Äüë®üèª and‚Ä¶ actually that‚Äôs kinda hard to read, lets add some white space

ü¶Äüë®üèª yeah‚Ä¶ no that didn‚Äôt help

ü¶Äüë®üèª Anyway, while this is hello world, so is this

ü¶Äüë®üèª In the official hello world, loops are used to increment values, but in this version, we‚Äôre just adding to the current memory location

ü¶Äüë®üèª exporting it

ü¶Äüë®üèª and moving to the next memory location.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Let‚Äôs turn this into a test

ü¶Äüë®üèª I made this version of Hello World because it only uses three characters out of the eight used in brain fudge, so to make this test pass, we only need to implement three operations

ü¶Äüë®üèª So now we have a test, lets work out how to handle greater than, add, and period.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We‚Äôll create a new helper macro that can handle each of these tokens, by having a rule that matches each specific token.

ü¶Äüë®üèª These rules will be called recursively so we will also need a special rule to handle when there are no tokens left so we have an endpoint to the recursion.

ü¶Äüë®üèª To make it work, each rule will need to access the memory, the pointer, and the output buffer, so we‚Äôll add an expectation of those to the match pattern.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Additionally, because each rule only handles one instruction, we need to pass the rest of the instructions to the macro again recursively.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Unlike our earlier examples, we‚Äôre going to use a semicolon as a separator in our pattern matching.

ü¶Äüë®üèª The reason for this is that Brain Fudge uses commas as part of its syntax.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We would still be able to correctly parse Brain Fudge code as, even were the first token in a program a comma, it still comes after the last comma we match against, but it will help with readability for anyone using the macro.

ü¶Äüë®üèª So here we go.

ü¶Äüë®üèª First lets handle plus token.

ü¶Äüë®üèª All we need to do is add one to the value in memory at the pointer.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Brain Fudge is non-specific about what to do in the event of an overflows, so I‚Äôm going to choose to allow it to wrap around

ü¶Äüë®üèª Next lets implement the rule for the greater than token

ü¶Äüë®üèª In this case we‚Äôre increment the value of the pointer but this time I don‚Äôt want to wrap.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We‚Äôre using saturating_add for the specific reason we want to be consistent and I don‚Äôt want to wrap a usize on subtract, you‚Äôll see why later!

ü¶Äüë®üèª We also need to make sure that any time we go out of bounds of the memory Vec, that we resize the Vec appropriately and zero memory

ü¶Äüë®üèª We can do this with a quick loop, pushing 0‚Äôs onto the end

ü¶Äüë®üèª Next we want to match the dot operator.

ü¶Äüë®üèª It takes the value at the current pointer and writes it to our output buffer

ü¶Äüë®üèª Finally, we have a match arm for when there are no more tokens left that doesn‚Äôt do anything.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Now we can update our brain_fudge! macro to call the helper, passing in the program state.

ü¶Äüë®üèª So we now have a program that has our brain fudge macro, a brain fudge helper macro that passes 3 of the 8 tokens, and a test‚Ä¶

ü¶Äüë®üèª when build the program

ü¶Äüë®üèª we get this error

ü¶Äüë®üèª Rust keeps track of how many times we recurse, that is, call a function or macro from the same function or macro.

ü¶Äüë®üèª By default, the maximum amount of times we can do this is 128.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Our macro, when parsing our silly Hello World example, recurses 1120 times!

ü¶Äüë®üèª So, we could avoid recursing by looping through the tokens instead, and that will work for this current instruction set‚Ä¶ but it won‚Äôt work for loops.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We probably could come up with a way of mixing loops and recursion but to keep things simple, we‚Äôre going to play a dangerous game and manually tell Rust it‚Äôs fine for it to recurse 2048 times.

ü¶Äüë®üèª The recursion_limit attribute applies at the crate level so be careful with this one!

ü¶Äüë®üèª And now our code runs!

We‚Äôve made a great start, we‚Äôve got almost half the language done already, and I think, hope(?), you‚Äôll agree this wasn‚Äôt all that painful.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Lets implement some of the other operations.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Dealing with less than and minus is easy enough, they‚Äôre the opposite of what we already have.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Minus similar to Add, does a wrapping subtract of the value at the pointer

ü¶Äüë®üèª Less than does a saturating subtract of our pointer.

ü¶Äüë®üèª This is where that saturating behaviour matters; were we to wrap it, 0 - 1 becomes a number so large I can‚Äôt even say it.

ü¶Äüë®üèª More complex is the loop.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Luckily, we aren‚Äôt dealing with individual tokens, we‚Äôre dealing with token trees!

ü¶Äüë®üèª In Rust, these bracket pairs specifically are all considered tokens that wrap other tokens.

ü¶Äüë®üèª They‚Äôre treated as a single token tree that contain more token trees.

ü¶Äüë®üèª So Rust will correctly handle them in pairs, even when nested.

ü¶Äüë®üèª This means to make our loop rule work, we can match against any token tree that starts with an open square bracket, contains more tokens which may include more square bracket pairs, and matches its ending close square bracket.

ü¶Äüë®üèª This is followed by yet more tokens!

ü¶Äüë®üèª How cool is it that we got this for free!?

ü¶Äüë®üèª The loop is really easy to implement too!

ü¶Äüë®üèª We check the value at the current pointer position, if it‚Äôs not zero we run the code in the loop and check again.

ü¶Äüë®üèª If it is zero then we continue recursing with the rest of the code just like every other rule.

ü¶Äüë®üèª To make sure our new code works we‚Äôll update our test with the ‚Äúreal‚Äù Hello World.

ü¶Äüë®üèª And when we build‚Ä¶ it doesn‚Äôt work again

ü¶Äüë®üèª It actually gives us a number of errors, all different, all valid, but I‚Äôm only showing the first because it tells us what we need to know

ü¶Äüë®üèª Why is it pointing at two greater than signs?

ü¶Äüë®üèª We have a match on greater than already, right?

Well here‚Äôs the problem with using tokens for our DSL.

Rust considers two greater than symbols to be a single token.

Specifically it‚Äôs a ‚Äúright shift‚Äù operator.

Tokens in Rust can be multiple characters.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Here are our problem tokens and what they mean in each language:

ü¶Äüë®üèª ‚Äúdot, dot‚Äù in Rust is a range literal, but in Brain Fudge it just means output the current value twice.

ü¶Äüë®üèª ‚Äúgreater than, greater than‚Äù in Rust is a right shift operator, but in Brain Fudge it means increment the pointer twice

ü¶Äüë®üèª ‚Äúless than, less than‚Äù in Rust is a left shift operator, but in Brain Fudge it means decrement the pointer twice

ü¶Äüë®üèª ‚Äúdash, greater than‚Äù in Rust is used when writing function return types, in Brain Fudge it means decrement the value at the pointer then increment the pointer

ü¶Äüë®üèª ‚Äúless than, dash‚Äù in Rust isn‚Äôt used currently, but is reserved, in Brain Fudge it means decrement the pointer then decrement the value

Soooo‚Ä¶ unfortunately we need to take care of these special cases.

Fortunately, while ‚Äúgreater than, greater than‚Äù is a right shift token, ‚Äúgreater than, space, greater than‚Äù is two greater than tokens.

Tokens can be seperated by whitespace which is ignored by the token tree fragment-specifier

All we need to do is split the token and pass it back into the macro

Now when we run our code it works!

And we just created a transpiler for another language inside Rust!

That‚Äôs kind of wild, right?!
