Unsafe Rust - open 1

Today we‚Äôre going to get unsafe, and this shirt is entirely too safe calp 4 x

open 2

Arguably, the main reason you‚Äôd want to pick up Rust over any other compiled language is that Rust keeps you safe, but what does that mean?

When you write a program in a compiled high-level language like Rust, C, C++, Zig, et cetera, it‚Äôs converted into low-level instructions for processors.

These instructions are very direct, they move data from one register to another, add, subtract, shift bits and so on

open 4

There are no restrictions on what those low-level instructions can do, which means you can do things that might result in unexpected or undefined behaviour.

This is what we consider to be ‚Äúunsafe‚Äù.

For example, in low-level languages that instruct processors directly, there‚Äôs nothing stopping you reading and writing to the same memory location at the same time from different threads.

open 5

Using any high-level language allows us to abstract this behavior in ways that are easier for us to understand.

All compiled languages go further than just making the instructions easier to read and write.

They also enforce their own rules as to how they‚Äôre used.

open 6

Rust with its ownership rules and borrow checker is just one example of how compiled languages make sure that we, the software engineers, don‚Äôt ask the computer to do anything that could be unsafe.

To the best of its ability, Rust will check that your code is safe.

But‚Ä¶ if it only let you do things it could prove are safe, you‚Äôd be cut off from making a lot of programs.

open 7

This series is accompanied by a free book, check the description for a link straight to this chapter.

My name is Daniel, welcome to IRISS.

Intro - intro 1

Sometimes when we use Rust, we need to access heap memory, use peripherals, and talk to other software, or even dip into those specific processor instructions we just mentioned.

We can‚Äôt guarantee that any of these actions are safe.

intro 2

But wait! Haven‚Äôt we been accessing Heap Memory throughout this series?

Why yes, yes we have.

intro 3

Types like Vec and String use the Heap to store their data.

They take responsibility for, and abstract away, any unsafe work, meaning that using those types is safe.

intro 4

As a Rust engineer, depending on the type of project you‚Äôre working on, most of the time you won‚Äôt personally need to worry about unsafe Rust.

You can get by with using other people‚Äôs code and APIs like the standard library.

The point of this video isn‚Äôt to prepare you to write lots of unsafe Rust; it‚Äôs to make you feel comfortable for the odd occasion you might have to touch it.

Recap on Memory - recap 1

We often talk about memory in programming as if it‚Äôs a single thing, but our programs subdivide memory into three main types of usages, each of which has different pros and cons.

recap 2

Stack Memory is where all variables live‚Ä¶ kind of.

It‚Äôs fast because the entire stack is pre-allocated when your program runs, but there are some catches.

recap 3

First, it‚Äôs actually quite limited.

When your program starts, it‚Äôs given an amount of memory that you cannot specify ahead of time, and you cannot change.

It‚Äôs usually two Mebibytes, but you might find it‚Äôs even less on targets such as embedded devices.

If you use more than this small amount of memory, your program will crash.

recap 4

Second, the data stored on the stack must be of known size at compile time.

You don‚Äôt really need to worry about why this is only that data on the stack cannot change in size.

recap 5

But we‚Äôve stored lots of things in variables that have variable size, right? Strings, Vecs, HashMaps, etc.

recap 6

The data for these types is not stored in the variable.

What typically happens is that data we care about is stored on the Heap, and that data‚Äôs location, and some other metadata (all of which is of known size) is stored on the stack.

recap 7

Semantically, it‚Äôs probably fine to say that the variable contains that data; people will always know what you mean.

However, for this video, we really need to differentiate what is on the stack and what isn‚Äôt.

recap 8

ü¶Äüë®üèª In this code, number_example stores the actual number on the stack, since its of a known size, 32 bits unless otherwise specified.

ü¶Äüë®üèª string_example, however, contains the location of the string data, not the data itself, which is stored on the heap.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We‚Äôll talk about how it gets to the heap shortly.

recap 9

The Heap is where we can store things of arbitrary size, and we can (usually) store things far larger than the entire stack there.

Heap memory is allocated on request and freed once you‚Äôre done using it.

Technically, we can‚Äôt resize heap allocations either; but we can request new, larger portions of heap memory, copy our data there, free the old memory, and add more things in the new location until we run out of space again.

recap 10

So it‚Äôs bigger and more flexible than the stack, but it also has some downsides.

It‚Äôs much slower than stack memory because allocating and freeing the memory takes time.

Allocation and Freeing in Rust is usually handled by the standard library and, other than what we‚Äôre going to discuss in this chapter, you almost never need to think about that process, but it‚Äôs not free.

recap 11

Sidebar:

Once heap memory is allocated, it‚Äôs almost free to use, with the only overhead essentially being the redirection from the stack to the heap in O(1) time.

For this reason, some software and languages will actually allocate large amounts of memory called Page Files that can store lots of different things.

This can be done in Rust too, and again there are pros and cons, but it‚Äôs far outside the scope of this video.

recap 12

There‚Äôs a third kind of memory we don‚Äôt really talk about as much, but it might be the most important.

Static Memory is where all values and data you write into your program are initially stored, though frequently it‚Äôs subsequently moved somewhere else.

recap 13

ü¶Äüë®üèª For example, in this program, the data for CONST_EXAMPLE remains in static memory.

ü¶Äüë®üèª But CONST_EXAMPLE itself is a reference to that data, see the ampersand.

ü¶Äüë®üèª 42 and "This is string data too" are also initially stored in static data.

ü¶Äüë®üèª However, 42 is copied to the stack in number_example whereas "This is string data too" is cloned onto the heap and the location of the data is stored in string_example.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Differentiating where things are stored is about to become very important, and cough it‚Äôs easy to make mistakes if we don‚Äôt differentiate between the stack, the heap and static memory.

Not really all that unsafe - not unsafe 01

It‚Äôs important to note that Unsafe Rust doesn‚Äôt turn off any of Rusts safety measures.

What it does do is turn on a whole other set of language features on which Rusts usual safety measures cannot work.

not unsafe 02

I really can‚Äôt stress this enough as it might be one of the greatest misconceptions about Rust.

Unsafe Rust does not turn off any safety measures.

It turns on tools that Rust cannot guarantee are safe, so you need to make extra certain you are using them safely.

not unsafe 03

For example, in safe Rust we use references.

These are similar to pointers in other languages which point to a location in memory, but references are not pointers.

The validity of a pointer cannot be checked, but in Rust, a reference abides by rules that guarantee its validity at compile time before the program even runs.

In unsafe Rust we can access pointers too, but References‚Ä¶ must still abide by the rules of the borrow checker.

not unsafe 04

Most of these tools exist in other commonly used compiled languages that have been around for decades and are still, rightly, very popular today.

In many of these languages, these tools are available at any time.

not unsafe 05

Having the tools is not a bad thing.

not unsafe 06

They‚Äôre necessary tools that we use to do things that there is no other way to do.

By making you explicitly opt in to these tools, though, Rust forces you to really think about how you‚Äôre using them.

How to use unsafe - how 01

ü¶Äüë®üèª Any time we use unsafe code we need to wrap it inside an unsafe block.

ü¶Äüë®üèª This code uses an unsafe block to call a function that is itself marked as unsafe.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Because the function is marked as unsafe it can only be called within unsafe code.

ü¶Äüë®üèª However, even within that function, code is treated as safe until you use another unsafe block.

ü¶Äüë®üèª This helps constrain unsafe code further

ü¶Äüë®üèª We‚Äôll talk about why you might mark functions as unsafe further on.

ü¶Äüë®üèª But‚Ä¶ what‚Äôs with all the comments?

how 02

ü¶Ä This is not necessarily a widely used practice, but maybe it should be

ü¶Ä The Rust Standard Library team, who have to work with unsafe a lot, have standardized around making safety communication almost contractual.

ü¶Ä Prior to the unsafe block, the first thing we see is a SAFETY: comment.

ü¶Ä This tells the reader how the author made sure this code was safe‚Ä¶ this may seem odd.

how 03

ü¶Ä If the code is provably safe, why do we need unsafe at all?

ü¶Ä unsafe turns on language features that can‚Äôt be proven safe by the compiler, but that‚Äôs no excuse for writing unsafe code unsafely.

how 04

ü¶Ä The # Safety section of the doc comment on the function this_code_is_unsafe is used to tell consumers of that function how to use the function safely.

ü¶Ä What you‚Äôll often find is that SAFETY: comments should mirror # Safety doc comments, to show that the code follows the guidelines laid out.

ü¶Ä We‚Äôll talk more about unsafe functions later.

how 05

The practice of writing a SAFETY: comment ensures that when we write unsafe code, we think hard about how we know this code isn‚Äôt going to hurt us later.

Documenting how we know this code is safe is crucial.

You can read more about this practice in the official Standard Library Developer‚Äôs Guide linked in the description

Raw Pointers - raw 01

One of the main reasons you might want to dip into unsafe Rust is to communicate with memory directly.

Rust‚Äôs abstractions around memory are powerful, usually free (or otherwise cheap) and very flexible, but they don‚Äôt cover every possible need.

You may have a use case where you need to talk to memory without going through Rust‚Äôs abstractions, and Rust lets you do that by turning on its unsafe features to access raw pointers.

raw 02

We usually use References in Rust as if they were pointers, pointing to something that‚Äôs actually stored elsewhere.

But while References include features that make them safer to use, a pointer is essentially just a number that is an address to a location in memory.

raw 03

When you allocate heap data, even in Rust, the operating system provides you with a pointer to the location where the memory was allocated.

If we just used a pointer, it would still contain the numeric address of that location even if we subsequently told the operating system to free that memory.

raw 04

Programmatically, we have no way to know if that location is still ours to use later.

Using that pointer after the memory is freed would be an error, and is the root of an extremely common bug you might have heard of called ‚Äúuse after free‚Äù.

In fact, because we don‚Äôt know from just the pointer whether the memory was freed or not, we might even try to free the memory again, leading to another bug called ‚Äúdouble free‚Äù.

raw 05

References help us avoid that because we can track their use at compile time, helping us make sure that they are always valid before we even run the code‚Ä¶

but the operating system doesn‚Äôt use references.

raw 06

Actually, references can‚Äôt be used between any two separate pieces of software, because of their compile time nature.

We can, however, share pointer locations.

raw 07

So, even in Rust, pointers are being used all the time, whether we see them or not.

Sometimes, we may even need to use pointers directly ourselves.

raw 08

ü¶Äüë®üèª You can actually get pointers in safe Rust, using ‚Äúampersand raw const‚Äù or for pointers to mutable data, ‚Äúampersand raw mut‚Äù.

ü¶Äüë®üèª If we run this program several times, you‚Äôll see we get a different number every time

ü¶Äüë®üèª One cool thing worth pointing out is that Rust even types your pointers, including whether the data there is mutable, making it harder to mess things up later.

ü¶Äüë®üèª An immutable pointer to an i32 has the type *const i32

raw 09

Remember, in some circumstances, the variable that you‚Äôre accessing the data through does not contain the actual data.

Strings are a good example of this.

raw 10

ü¶Äüë®üèª The pointer to the variable does not point to the string data, it points to metadata which itself contains a pointer to the string data.

ü¶Äüë®üèª We can access the pointer to the data via a method on the String itself.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Again, there‚Äôs nothing unsafe about doing this; we‚Äôre just reading a numeric value that is definitely valid at the time we do it.

raw 11

So getting pointers is perfectly safe.

What we can‚Äôt do is use those pointers to get the data stored at that location in safe Rust.

For that we need to dip into unsafe.

raw 12

ü¶Äüë®üèª Here we dereference the pointer to go back from the location to the data that‚Äôs stored there.

ü¶Äüë®üèª This is unsafe because Rust cannot confirm the validity of the pointer when we do it, it‚Äôs just some number.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Without us checking manually, pointer could outlive the_answer, after which, what is pointer pointing at?

Unsafe functions - fn 1

When we write code, we regularly break it up into small reusable chunks known as functions.

fn 2

You are, at this point, I hope, very familiar with this idea.

fn 3

So far we‚Äôve demonstrated that we can place unsafe code inside a block to encapsulate unsafe behavior.

This means that you can write unsafe code inside a function, as long as you write the function to make sure that there‚Äôs no risk, meaning, for someone calling the function, that function is safe.

fn 4

ü¶Äüë®üèª A good example of this is ‚Äústud mem swap‚Äù which swaps the values at two mutable locations:

ü¶Äüë®üèª Because swap guarantees the types are the same and, through using mutable references, knows nothing else is accessing the memory while it does its thing, conceptually this function is safe, even if the first thing it does internally is run unsafe code.

ü¶Äüë®üèª This is what we call a safe abstraction around unsafe code.

fn 5

But that‚Äôs not always possible.

Sometimes, the very concept a function or method represents is unsafe.

fn 6

Let‚Äôs say that through arbitrary means, we‚Äôve got a pointer to some heap data that we know represents a String.

We know how long the String is and how much memory at that location is ours. We want to take ownership of that memory and turn it into a String type.

fn 7

üìï We can use the from_raw_parts static method to build a String directly from memory, but the entire concept of manually creating a String like this is unsafe.

üìï Firstly, something else likely manages that Heap memory.

üìï If we create a String from it, we‚Äôre going to take joint ownership of the heap data, and when our String goes out of scope, Rust will try to free it.

üìï How do we prevent a double free when the thing that originally created the data also wants to free it?

üìï Secondly, from_raw_parts takes a pointer, a length, and a capacity, none of which can be validated at compile time.

üìï And, String will cause a reallocation if the capacity is filled, causing us another problem to look out for!

fn 8

ü¶Ä Luckily, by being aware of the problems ahead of time, we can still use this function safely.

ü¶Ä We‚Äôll manually make sure our string never exceeds 100 bytes

ü¶Ä Little side note: The string may be created with more than 100 bytes, but we will own the entire actual capacity and need to free it all when we‚Äôre done with it.

ü¶Ä from_raw_parts is an unsafe method so can only be called inside an unsafe block

ü¶Ä I‚Äôm intentionally calling it with a length less than the original string to make a point

ü¶Ä Before we do anything else, we‚Äôre going to prevent overlapping_string from being dropped, which would otherwise cause a double free when original_string is dropped later.

ü¶Ä We could prevent original_string being dropped instead, but, to me, it makes sense to have this behavior in the inner code block.

ü¶Ä Counter intuitively, the ManuallyDrop type is actually safe.

ü¶Ä Although it prevents the memory being freed, and could result in memory leaks, that‚Äôs not considered unsafe in the same way as other things in this video.

ü¶Ä Just be careful using it.

ü¶Ä Here we‚Äôre going to check that the String is what we expect it to be.

ü¶Ä Due to giving it a smaller length, we only see the first part of the original string.

ü¶Ä We can push data onto the end of the string, and it will correctly change the string.

ü¶Ä But look how we massacred our boy!

fn 9

It‚Äôs not unusual to create an unsafe function but then wrap that function in safe code.

Like the example we used earlier: ‚Äústud mem swap‚Äù is safe, even though it does unsafe things.

fn 10

ü¶Äüë®üèª Creating safe abstractions might look something like this

ü¶Äüë®üèª We have a conceptually unsafe function that does some work.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Ideally, we‚Äôd create a safe abstraction, a function that is safe to call by doing whatever necessary checks.

ü¶Äüë®üèª The safe abstraction can then be called from safe code.

üìï stud mem swap does this simply by making sure you pass in mutable references of the same type, that‚Äôs enough to guarantee safety.

fn 11

When it comes to traits, if any of a trait‚Äôs methods are unsafe, then the entire trait is considered unsafe, and so is its implementation.

It‚Äôs actually kind of rare to have to use this feature.

If your trait has an unsafe method but a safe abstraction, you might be able to move the unsafe method to an unsafe function.

fn 12

For example, this trait has two provided methods, but we still can‚Äôt implement it safely, even with the default implementations.

We can implement it with the unsafe keyword though:

However, if we don‚Äôt need to ever override the unsafe method, we could just extract it from the trait entirely

fn 10

You‚Äôre likely to need unsafe Traits only when the behavior the trait describes is itself is unsafe.

For example, Send and Sync are automatically applied to all types that are only constructed from types that are also Send and Sync, but both traits are conceptually unsafe.

If your type contains types that are not Send and/or Sync then the compiler can no longer guarantee safe implementation itself.

You can still implement Send and Sync for your type manually but its now up to you to check the implementation is safe, so implementing the traits themselves is unsafe.

Unions - unions 01

Unions, in software engineering, are a way of storing different types in the same section of memory.

They‚Äôre typically broken into two flavors, tagged and untagged.

unions 02

‚ÄúTagged‚Äù simply means the type is part of data, so you can only access the data as the type that it is. We use tagged unions in Rust all the time, and they‚Äôre perfectly safe:

unions 03

ü¶Äüë®üèª Enums are tagged unions

ü¶Äüë®üèª They only ever take up as much memory as is taken by the largest data type representable inside them, plus a discrimination value which differentiates the variants at runtime

ü¶Äüë®üèª The discrimination value is usually represented as an isize but Rust compilers may use smaller numeric types

unions 04

But Rust also has ‚Äúuntagged‚Äù unions, where the type being used is not part of the data, and you can access the binary value as any listed type.

Untagged unions are obviously unsafe, but they provide several useful features, either by allowing you to interrogate the data in different ways, or for working with other programming languages that use untagged unions.

unions 05

Side Note

My first attempt at an example for demonstrating untagged unions was an IPv4 address union that used both a 32bit integer, and a four-byte array

However, with that example we have to consider ‚Äúendianness‚Äù which is the order in which bytes are stored in memory.

This felt like it went too far off-topic, however, it‚Äôs still worth pointing out that when creating unions that share multiple bytes of data, you too may need to consider endianness.

unions 06

ü¶Äüë®üèª In this example, we can interrogate characters as u32‚Äôs (characters in Rust are four bytes, although most string types use a variable byte width encoding such as utf-8).

ü¶Äüë®üèª Creating unions is safe:

ü¶Äüë®üèª But, reading unions is unsafe.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Even in this case where both u32 and char are 32 bits wide, not all valid u32 values are valid chars, but all chars are valid u32s

ü¶Äüë®üèª Writing values is also safe though.

ü¶Äüë®üèª When we run the program, we can see that both character and number change

Assembly - asm 1

This next example of unsafe is so incredibly unsafe the only time you‚Äôre ever likely to use it is if you need insane speed and know exactly what you‚Äôre doing with the exact hardware you‚Äôre targeting.

asm 2

You might have heard of assembly, but crucially, it‚Äôs not one language.

Assembly languages are languages that have a near 1:1 relationship with the actual instructions of the CPU you‚Äôre building for.

asm 3

ü¶Ä In this example you can see a function that takes a number and multiplies it by 6 using assembly.

ü¶Ä There are two versions of the function:

ü¶Ä The first works on ‚Äúx86_64‚Äù targets, which cover most Windows and Linux machines and older Macs.

ü¶Ä The second works on ‚Äúaarch64‚Äù targets, which cover all modern Macs but also some newer Windows and Linux machines.

ü¶Ä As you can see, apart from mov, the other instructions look very different but do the same things.

ü¶Ä For obvious reasons, Rust cannot help keep you safe when you‚Äôre sending instructions straight to hardware, so assembly is only available within unsafe code.

asm 4

Of all Rust‚Äôs unsafe features, this is the one you‚Äôre least likely to need to touch, but, as with the others, it‚Äôs there if you need it.

extern - extern 1

A lot of the time you‚Äôre going to be working with other peoples code.

Most often, that code will be written in Rust, downloaded as a crate, and combined with your code into a single binary.

extern 2

Sometimes, though, you‚Äôll want to work with code written in other languages, either by consuming a library written in another language, or by building a library that another language can consume.

This is done with extern.

extern 3

For compiled languages to interoperate, they need to use an Application Binary Interface.

Rust doesn‚Äôt (and probably never will) have a stable ABI.

Instead, we use typically use C‚Äôs, though other options also exist.

extern 4

Creating a library that can be consumed by other languages is fairly safe.

You define a function that will be made available extern-ally (thus the name).

The function itself is safe‚Ä¶ exposing it is not.

To be made available, the name of the function has to be known.

extern 5

Rust usually ‚Äúmangles‚Äù its function names to avoid what‚Äôs called symbol collisions, where two things have the same name.

To expose the function, we need to know its name, so we need to prevent the mangling, but that means there‚Äôs a risk of collision.

extern 6

ü¶Äüë®üèª Pre Rust-2024 preventing mangling was done with the #[no_mangle] attribute, but as of Rust 2024, we need to explicitly tell Rust that we understand the dangers.

ü¶Äüë®üèª There‚Äôs one more thing to consider, though. Our code will be called by code that we can‚Äôt control.

ü¶Äüë®üèª You probably want to make sure you validate data coming from eternal code, especially for anything more complex than a number.

extern 7

ü¶Äüë®üèª You can also call functions from other libraries if they use a supported ABI too.

ü¶Äüë®üèª You do this by linking an extern block to a specific library and then listing the signatures of the functions exposed by that library that you want to use in your code.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Everything about this is unsafe.

ü¶Äüë®üèª If there‚Äôs no dynamic library on the target system that shares a name with the linked library, the program will panic.

ü¶Äüë®üèª If the function names do not match, the program will panic.

ü¶Äüë®üèª If the function names match but the signature doesn‚Äôt, you‚Äôll get undefined behavior, or it will panic.

ü¶Äüë®üèª Nonetheless, the ability to import libraries written in other languages can be a powerful tool in your arsenal.

Mutable Statics - statics 1

In older editions of Rust, it was possible to have mutable statics via unsafe Rust.

This would allow you to have mutable global variables that were safe to use so long as they weren‚Äôt shared between threads.

As of Rust 2024, this is no longer the case.

statics 2

If you don‚Äôt mind sacrificing a tiny bit of speed, the same thing can be achieved through safe abstractions and tools like atomic types or interior mutability.

If you really need the speed, then you can still fall back on raw pointers.

At the end of the day, though, mutable globals are a bit of a crutch and should generally be avoided where possible, and I think there‚Äôs a lesson here.

statics 3

Any time you find yourself reaching for unsafe code, you want to be sure that there‚Äôs no other acceptable way to do the same thing.

Unsafe Rust can speed up your code and can let you do things there‚Äôs no other way of doing, but you should assess the various possibilities and trade-offs of alternatives before commiting to unsafe.

Even if you have to use unsafe code, are there existing safe abstractions for what you‚Äôre attempting in the standard library or well-tested crates?

You shouldn‚Äôt be afraid of unsafe, but you should understand that it is going to turn on features that you are responsible for making sure are used safely.

If you don‚Äôt need them, don‚Äôt use them.

Summary - summary 1

Outside of specialist use cases, you‚Äôre unlikely to have to write much, if any, unsafe code yourself.

summary 2

Nonetheless, hopefully after this chapter you see that it‚Äôs not as scary as it seems.

You still have all the normal safety checks, but now you have additional features you can‚Äôt get otherwise

You now know a bit more about what to look for to keep yourself safe when the compiler can no longer help.

summary 3

If you are going to be writing unsafe Rust, there‚Äôs a tool called Miri that will, in your running code, help you detect potentially undefined behavior you might have missed.

It‚Äôs not a panacea, it can‚Äôt catch everything, but it is a final potential safety net you should use to protect yourself.

We‚Äôre going to lean into pretty much everything we‚Äôve learned so far to learn async Rust.

This is going to be a bit of a unique chapter.

next 2

We‚Äôll go deeper than you generally need to go, in our exploration of the space.

Typically, you‚Äôd grab a crate like Tokio or AsyncStd to do all the hard stuff for you.

next 3

As with this video, you may never need to write any of the complex stuff, but‚Ä¶

It should help you feel comfortable with what I think many would agree is the last remaining truly sharp edge of Rust programming.

next 4

Async Rust

open 1

Often, when we write code, we have to wait on certain ‚Äúthings that take time‚Äù to happen.

‚ÄúThings that take time‚Äù are usually things like IO, such as reading or writing to a disk, getting data from a database, downloading a file, and so on.

Sometimes ‚Äúthings that take time‚Äù could just be computationally expensive, such as processing large amounts of data.

open 2

Usually our programs are complex with multiple parts to them.

For example, applications often have a user interface, whether that‚Äôs via a terminal, a web page, or a full desktop GUI.

If our application has to do ‚Äúthings that take time‚Äù, we don‚Äôt want other aspects (like the UI) to freeze up, unable to do anything while we wait.

Asynchronous programming is an implementation-independent way to structure our code so that we can continue to do work while waiting on other things.

open 3

This series is accompanied by a free book, check the description for a link straight to this chapter.

My name is Daniel, welcome to IRISS.

Intro

intro 1

Asynchronous programming doesn‚Äôt necessarily mean threads.

Some languages that support async programming don‚Äôt even support multiple threads.

Under the hood, we might use threads, or we may depend on Operating System hooks, or even, for embedded programming, we might directly depend on hardware interrupts and exceptions.

intro 2

In this video we‚Äôre going to explore the modular design of async programming in Rust.

Our aim is to get a solid(ish) understanding of the concepts involved, even though, in the real world, you‚Äôre likely to depend on others to design and build the more complex parts of the system.

Move and Pin

pin 1

Before we jump into the main part of the video, as ever, we once again need to talk about memory, specifically: moving and pinning.

All data in our running program exists somewhere in memory, whether it‚Äôs the stack, the heap, or static memory.

That means that everything has a pointer address in memory.

pin 2

When we pass a variable to another function, ownership of that variable ‚Äúmoves‚Äù to the other function.

When we add a variable to a Vec then ownership of that variable ‚Äúmoves‚Äù to the heap.

Any time data ‚Äúmoves‚Äù ownership, it also physically moves in memory.

pin 3

ü¶Ä We can see this in action with some simple code and some knowledge about pointers from our previous video.

ü¶Ä We‚Äôll create a String type and give it to the variable greet.

ü¶Ä As we know from last time, the data we care about, in this case ‚ÄúHello, world!‚Äù will exist on the Heap.

ü¶Ä The variable greet contains some metadata about where our String is in the Heap, but greet is stored on the stack.

ü¶Ä We can get the pointer address to greet on the stack like this.

ü¶Ä Now lets move greet to another function, and get the new greets pointer.

ü¶Ä When we run the program, we get two similar, but different addresses.

pin 4

This is usually fine, but occasionally things might need to know where they themselves are in memory.

This is called self-referencing.

If something references itself, and we move it, where does that reference now point?

pin 5

ü¶Ä Let‚Äôs create a completely ridiculous struct that contains some data and a pointer to that data.

ü¶Ä When we create the struct, we‚Äôll need to set the pointer to None because the constructor itself will move the data.

ü¶Ä Instead, we‚Äôll set the pointer via a method after it‚Äôs been moved into place.

ü¶Ä Finally, we‚Äôll do the really stupid thing and dereference the pointer into the value.

ü¶Ä Let‚Äôs not forget that safety comment!

ü¶Ä That‚Äôs better.

pin 6

ü¶Ä Let‚Äôs write some code to instantiate our example struct‚Ä¶ and get that pointer set up.

ü¶Ä When we read the value via the pointer, it, perhaps surprisingly, works as expected!

ü¶Ä We can also change the value directly, and the pointer correctly gives us that updated value.

ü¶Ä Now let‚Äôs move ownership, and, yes, this is actually enough to do so.

ü¶Ä We‚Äôre technically moving it within the same stack frame, but this is a new variable even if it has the same name.

ü¶Ä Updating the value works, but reading it now gives us the old value.

ü¶Ä pointer_to_value is just a number pointing at a location in memory.

ü¶Ä We moved the data, but the pointer is still pointing at the old location.

pin 7

Self-referential data is dangerous‚Ä¶ but it can also be useful in certain circumstances.

Not in the example I just gave, but still.

For this reason, some Generic types occasionally need to take it into consideration.

To keep ourselves safe, we can ‚Äúpin‚Äù arbitrary data to memory, preventing it from being moved.

pin 10

We use the Pin type to express this behavior.

Pin used to confuse me, but I actually don‚Äôt think it‚Äôs doing anything remotely clever.

The type itself is just a container for a mutable reference, so the Pin itself is safe to move around

Through the magic of the borrow checker, holding that single mutable reference is enough to lock the data in place.

It‚Äôs more like a guard or guarantee than anything magical.

pin 11

ü¶Ä We can pin our example from earlier using Pin::new

ü¶Ä You can‚Äôt always use this, its kinda goofy, I‚Äôll explain in a minute

ü¶Ä We now can‚Äôt modify the original struct or move it because Pin is holding the mutable reference.

ü¶Ä But we can modify the data via the Pin thanks to Deref and DerefMut.

ü¶Ä We can also move the Pin and, unlike our earlier example, this works because the Pin is just a reference.

pin 12

üìï There‚Äôs a lot to Pin so and if you‚Äôre curious about it, the standard library documentation has a lot more information.

üìï For this video its enough to know that, in specific circumstances, like in modular asynchronous architecture where we don‚Äôt necessarily control everything, we need to be certain data won‚Äôt move unexpectedly, and this is achieved through the Pin type.

pin 13

Pin::new only works with types that are Unpin, which is the automatically implemented marker trait for types that‚Ä¶ don‚Äôt need to be pinned.

Told you it was goofy.

For things that do need to be pinned you can use things like Box::pin and the pin! macro which have their utility but, crucially‚Ä¶

‚Ä¶do move the data you‚Äôre trying to prevent moving, so take care of your references!

pin 14

That said, you would have to Box a pin if you want to return it doesn‚Äôt the stack.

In our example this is fine so long as we set the pointer after moving it into the Pin.

Breaking Down Work

tasks 1

üé® When we build software, we can compartmentalize different parts of our program into tasks.

üé® Imagine we want to download two websites and compare the contents.

üé® We download website A, then download website B, then compare the contents of the two sites.

üé® However, now that we‚Äôve broken it down into tasks, we can see the tasks for getting the websites don‚Äôt depend on each other and could be performed at the same time.

üé® Asynchronous design allows us to reason about our code at the task level.

üé® It doesn‚Äôt specifically tell us how that work will get done, though.

tasks 2

Different languages provide asynchronous utilities in different ways.

In C# the default way async tasks are run is using multiple threads in a thread pool.

Node.js is a single-threaded runtime, though, so async code uses operating system callbacks to let your program know when a task is complete.

tasks 3

Importantly, in both languages, async code ‚Äújust works‚Äù

You can start using it out of the box.

tasks 4

Rust provides ways to structure asynchronous code but doesn‚Äôt have a default way of making sure Asynchronous work is actually performed.

Arguably this makes async Rust harder to work with than other languages that support async fully out of the box.

However, this allows software engineers to choose the method of task execution that will work best for their application.

tasks 5

Over the rest of the video we‚Äôll go over the Rust way of thinking about asynchronous tasks and create our own way of getting the work described by those tasks to run asynchronously.

Tasks, Schedulers, Futures, and Executors

concepts 1

Asynchronous architectures allow us to break our work up so that we can process different bits of that work while waiting on other bits.

As discussed, conceptually, we break the work into tasks and then have some sort of scheduler that decides which task gets run when.

concepts 2

In Rust, we represent tasks with the Future trait, which can be applied to any type.

We manage task scheduling through what are usually called executors (or sometimes runtimes), which themselves use Wakers to decide when to run different tasks.

concepts 3

This sounds complicated, but by the end of this video, you‚Äôll hopefully have a reasonable idea of how Futures, executors, and Wakers work together, and if you don‚Äôt‚Ä¶ that‚Äôs actually ok.

concepts 4

Most of the time you won‚Äôt need to write any of these things yourself, but having even a vague understanding of them will help you write better async code, as well as spot and fix common issues you might run across.

Let‚Äôs get started by building up our understanding step by step.

Futures

futures 1

üìï The Future trait represents a task that may or may not be complete.

üìï Something that will be completed in the future, but not necessarily now.

üìï It has an associated type, Output and a single method, .poll().

üìï Output represents the type of the data eventually returned by the Future.

üìï Usually this type will actually be a Result<T, E> because if we‚Äôre waiting on something happening, there‚Äôs a chance that it might not.

üìï The .poll() method is a lot more interesting though.

futures 2 (cont image)

üìï Firstly, you‚Äôll notice that self is typed, which we haven‚Äôt seen in this series before.

üìï In Future‚Äôs, self is a Pinned mutable reference to data of the type the Future is applied to.

üìï The reason for this is where the Future executes might change, but because you might want to apply a Future to a self-referential type, we need to know the data the Future represents won‚Äôt itself move.

futures 3 (cont image)

üìï .poll() also takes a mutable reference to some sort of Context type.

üìï For now, the only thing Context contains is a Waker which we‚Äôll talk about later.

üìï The reason we don‚Äôt pass the Waker directly though is that in the future‚Ä¶ of Futures‚Ä¶ we might want to add more data to a Context.

futures 4

ü¶Ä Finally, the return type of the .poll() method is a Poll enum.

ü¶Ä The .poll() method should be called any time we want to make progress on a task.

ü¶Ä The return type tells us whether that call has resulted in an Output, represented by Poll::Ready(Self::Output), or if the poll is not currently complete and needs to be called again, represented by Poll::Pending.

futures 5

Once a Future has returned Ready, you shouldn‚Äôt call it again.

The official documentation explicitly says, calling poll after Ready may result in a panic.

We will be breaking this rule later, but we‚Äôll be very cautious when we do.

futures 6

ü¶Ä Let‚Äôs make our first Future then.

ü¶Ä We can apply the Future trait to anything, even a unit struct is fine.

ü¶Ä For now, our poll method will immediately return Poll::Ready with a static lifetime string slice reference.

futures 7

ü¶Ä We‚Äôll instantiate our unit struct, and immediately pin it.

ü¶Ä Next we‚Äôre going to create a context with a no-op Waker.

ü¶Ä Don‚Äôt worry about this yet, the ‚Äúnoop‚Äù Waker doesn‚Äôt do anything, and we‚Äôre not even using the context in this example.

ü¶Ä Unlike some languages, in Rust, Futures don‚Äôt do anything unless you call the .poll() method, so we‚Äôll do that and store the response

ü¶Ä We can then use a match to extract the output from the Poll enum

ü¶Ä Normally, Futures won‚Äôt be ‚Äúready‚Äù immediately, and will need to be polled again.

futures 8

ü¶Ä Let‚Äôs create a Future that doesn‚Äôt complete the first time you poll it, using a simple counter.

ü¶Ä Each time we call the .poll() method we‚Äôll check if we‚Äôre at zero by dereferencing the pin.

ü¶Ä If it‚Äôs zero, then we‚Äôre ready.

ü¶Ä If it‚Äôs not zero, we need to change the work remaining however, you‚Äôll notice that self is not mutable.

ü¶Ä This is where things get a little‚Ä¶ interesting.

ü¶Ä How you‚Äôll manage mutability in a Future you write yourself might depend on whether the Future needed to be pinned or not.

ü¶Ä If it did, you‚Äôll have to work out some kind of interior mutability mechanism for yourself.

ü¶Ä If it didn‚Äôt, then in all likelihood your type automatically implemented Unpin, which means you can safely(ish) take the mutable reference out of the Pin with .get_mut.

ü¶Ä This consumes the Pin but lets you mutate the data via the mutable reference.

ü¶Ä So here we can subtract from work remaining and return Poll::Pending

futures 9

ü¶Ä Once again we‚Äôll create our Future and pin it.

ü¶Ä Then we‚Äôll create a context that doesn‚Äôt do anything.

ü¶Ä When we call the .poll() method it consumes the Pin, but we can get a copy of the Pin with as_mut(), so long as our Pin is mutable

ü¶Ä Its a bit weird but easy enough to work with.

ü¶Ä Now poll the future until the work is done.

futures 10

So managing futures is just about repeatedly calling .poll() right?

Well‚Ä¶ no, not quite.

Executors

executors 1

ü¶Ä Let‚Äôs create a simple executor that will just poll Futures until they‚Äôre Ready.

ü¶Ä This executor takes a single generic Future and loops over the poll method

ü¶Ä As I mentioned earlier, we can only use Pin::new if the thing we‚Äôre pinning is Unpin.

ü¶Ä We could restrict our executor to only accept Futures that are also Unpin, but using the pin! macro will let us keep our generic interface simple and broad.

ü¶Ä Again, the pin! macro will cause the Future to move, but as long as no self-references have been formed yet, this should be fine.

ü¶Ä We‚Äôll create our noop waker and context.

ü¶Ä Next, we‚Äôll repeatedly call .poll() on the future until we have an output, which we instantly return from the loop with break, and from the executor via the loop.

executors 2

ü¶Ä This is all we need but for demonstrative purposes, lets add a bit of code to show how many times we called .poll().

ü¶Ä We‚Äôll start the counter at one, and add to it each time we get a Poll::Pending to get an accurate number of times we called .poll().

ü¶Ä We‚Äôll capture output here and return it at the end.

executors 3

ü¶Ä Passing our previous example in, this seems to work quite well.

ü¶Ä We called poll four times, once for each of the three fake ‚Äúwork‚Äús we needed to do, and once to get that final Poll::Ready.

executors 4

ü¶Ä But Futures usually wait on things like IO or heavy compute tasks which won‚Äôt nicely finish after a set number of calls.

ü¶Ä Let‚Äôs try mimicking that with a simple timer.

ü¶Ä We‚Äôll store a SystemTime after which our timer is considered to have elapsed, and we‚Äôll get that by instantiating the timer with a duration and adding it to the current time.

ü¶Ä When calling the .poll() method for the future implementation, we‚Äôll simply compare the current system time to the time after which we want to end and return Ready or Pending as appropriate.

ü¶Ä Let‚Äôs give it to our loop executor and run it.

ü¶Ä Woof.

executors 5

ü¶Ä Fifty million calls to the poll method‚Ä¶ in one second.

ü¶Ä I guess this speaks to the speed of Rust, but, wow are we doing a lot of nothing real quick here.

ü¶Ä This work is going to take resources like compute time away from other tasks, so we really want to avoid this.

ü¶Ä Rather than us polling the Future over and over, if only there was a way for the Future to let us know when it was ready to be polled again.

Waking

waking 1

Waker is a struct the Future can use to inform the executor it‚Äôs ready to have its .poll() method called again.

Before we get to that, though, we‚Äôre going to update our program to use threads for both tasks and scheduling.

It‚Äôs important to note, however, that threads are not a necessary element of asynchronous Rust.

There are other ways to achieve Waking, but for now, this is the approach we‚Äôll take.

waking 2

ü¶Ä First, let‚Äôs update our timer to use Threads.

ü¶Ä This is a bit more complex, so we‚Äôll talk through each bit.

ü¶Ä This time, we‚Äôll capture the Duration and save it for later.

ü¶Ä Once we start the thread we‚Äôll store its join handler in here.

ü¶Ä We won‚Äôt actually join the thread in the traditional sense, so to be honest, I‚Äôm not sure if this is necessary, I just didn‚Äôt like the idea of orphaning the thread.

ü¶Ä Next we‚Äôre finally going to use the Waker.

ü¶Ä We need to store it, and access if mutably from both the executor thread and the thread we‚Äôre creating, so into an Arc Mutex it goes.

ü¶Ä When we instantiate the ThreadTimer we won‚Äôt have a Waker yet, so we‚Äôll use the noop waker.

ü¶Ä This saves using an Option and is safe as we won‚Äôt start the timer until the first poll, at which point we‚Äôre given a real Waker.

ü¶Ä Finally, to mitigate a potential race condition we‚Äôre going to mark the Timer as complete, and again, this needs to be accessible from both threads, so this is also an Arc Mutex.

waking 3

ü¶Ä To implement the Future, first we need to access the Future mutably multiple times.

ü¶Ä We know it‚Äôs Unpin so we‚Äôre going to grab the mutable reference to the Timer out of the pin with get_mut.

ü¶Ä Next, we replace our Waker every time the .poll() method is called.

ü¶Ä Normally this will actually be the same Waker each time, but it is technically possible to move a Future from one executor to another, which might mean we need to use a different Waker.

ü¶Ä In the event we‚Äôre given a different Waker we‚Äôll store it every time .poll() is called.

ü¶Ä poll will be called multiple times, so if we haven‚Äôt started the thread yet, we should do that now.

ü¶Ä We‚Äôll take a copy of the Duration and clone the Arc‚Äôs for waker and is_complete so they can be moved into the thread.

ü¶Ä The thread itself will simply sleep itself for the duration, then set is_complete to true, then call the waker.

ü¶Ä Once the thread is set up, and on each subsequent call of .poll() we‚Äôll check if the Timer is complete and return the appropriate Poll variant.

waking 4

ü¶Ä Some important notes here.

ü¶Ä First, I‚Äôve used .unwrap() when getting the Mutex lock primarily to make this code easy to read but also because if either our inner or outer threads panic, the program is unrecoverable for reasons other than locking the mutex.

ü¶Ä Ideally you should at least use .expect(), or our Future could return a Result but we‚Äôll come to that later.

ü¶Ä Second, thread sleep is not remotely accurate.

ü¶Ä It‚Äôs guaranteed to sleep for at least as long as the duration, but might sleep for longer‚Ä¶ unless you give it a duration of Zero, in which case it might sleep, or it might not, depending on your system!

ü¶Ä Thirdly, there‚Äôs one more thing in this code that I‚Äôm hoping if you already use a lot of async in Rust, this has you cold sweats‚Ä¶ don‚Äôt worry, it‚Äôs safe, but we‚Äôll cover it at the end of the video. wink

waking 5

ü¶Ä Before we move on, lets test our new timer in our old loop executor.

ü¶Ä Now when we run it‚Ä¶ we hit .poll() 140 million times.

ü¶Ä Technically, I guess we start the timer a bit later (after calling .poll() and constructing a thread), but also our comparisons are a bit cheaper.

ü¶Ä Still, this is out of hand.

ü¶Ä Time to fix it once and for all.

waking 6

ü¶Ä First, we need a Waker.

ü¶Ä The Waker type itself is a concrete type, but when its created we can give it any type that implements the Wake trait.

ü¶Ä To stop the executor from polling unnecessarily, our plan is to simply pause the whole thread the executor is on; this is called ‚Äúparking‚Äù the thread.

ü¶Ä This makes our ThreadWaker fairly straightforward.

ü¶Ä We can create it with the current_thread() static method in which it takes a note of whatever thread it was created on.

ü¶Ä We implement the Wake trait for it, and when we call wake, all we do is ‚Äúunpark‚Äù that thread.

ü¶Ä Parking and Unparking is akin to pausing and unpausing threads.

waking 7

ü¶Ä So, onto the executor.

ü¶Ä We‚Äôve got the same signature as the loop executor, accepting any future and returning its output.

ü¶Ä We‚Äôll also pin the future before anything else, just like the loop executor.

ü¶Ä Our first real difference then is we now actually care about the Waker.

ü¶Ä We can create a Waker ‚Äúfrom‚Äù an Arc of any type that implements the Wake trait.

ü¶Ä By creating our ThreadWaker on the same thread as the executor, that‚Äôs the thread that gets saved for later.

ü¶Ä We‚Äôll then make our context from that waker.

ü¶Ä Again, all we need now is a loop over the future.

ü¶Ä If it‚Äôs ready, we‚Äôll break from the loop and return that value.

ü¶Ä If it‚Äôs not ready, we‚Äôll park the thread.

ü¶Ä When our ThreadTimer calls the Waker it unparks the thread, resuming the loop and causing the future to be called again.

ü¶Ä It could mean the Future is ready to progress, in which case it will return Pending again, or it could be Ready.

ü¶Ä Again, this is all that‚Äôs needed, but to show the improvement we‚Äôve made, lets add the counter back in

waking 8

ü¶Ä When we run our ThreadTimer in our new block_thread_on exectutor, you can see that our code is much more efficient with our poll method only being called twice.

ü¶Ä Once to start progressing the future, and once more to complete the future after the Waker is called.

ü¶Ä This means the program spends most of its one(ish) second runtime asleep, leaving resources like the CPU free to use.

Async / Await

async 1

The whole point of asynchronous code architectures is that we break our code down into small tasks.

Implementing our own Futures is great for the very edge of our Rust code where we‚Äôre waiting on some sort of I/O from outside our program, or something like a compute heavy task we control.

async 2

Most of the time we won‚Äôt necessarily even be doing that, though, as there are lots of crates for dealing with common I/O tasks, like reading files, accessing databases, or downloading files.

Most of the time, we just need to glue those bits together.

This is where async/await comes in.

async 3

ü¶Ä We can make any function or any block of code a Future with the async keyword.

ü¶Ä When you write async in front of a code block, that code block will be evaluated as a Future with an Output type that matches that of the block.

ü¶Ä In this example, we simply instantiate and return a String, so the async code block is a Future where the Output type is a String.

ü¶Ä To use this Future we can pass it straight into our executor.

ü¶Ä When you write async in front of a function, then the return value of calling that function is a Future with an Output type that matches the return type of the function.

ü¶Ä This example also returns a String but because the function is async, instead of getting a String when we call the function we get a Future where the Output type is a String.

ü¶Ä In this case we need to call the async function to get the Future to pass into our executor.

async 4

ü¶Ä But async code blocks and functions do something a little bit special.

ü¶Ä They can work up to another future and then pause until that future is ready to continue by using the .await postfix on a Future.

ü¶Ä What‚Äôs rather brilliant in async code, though, is that when waked? woken‚Ä¶ the code resumes from where it got to.

ü¶Ä Furthermore, .await will automatically unwrap the Poll enum for you, giving you the Poll::Ready value.

ü¶Ä Because our Timer‚Äôs don‚Äôt return any values, let‚Äôs have this async block called by another.

ü¶Ä When we run this code, we go into future_that_uses_that_value then into future_with_value, then into the ThreadTimer.

ü¶Ä The Waker from the executor is passed all the way into that future, and then the executor pauses until its told work can proceed.

ü¶Ä After we exit out of future_with_value we get the value.

ü¶Ä More importantly, because the value is considered to be instantly available after awaiting it, and because most of the time when waiting on something we have to assume that thing might fail‚Ä¶ we arrive at the most sublime pairing of Rust syntax, .await?.

async 5

ü¶Ä Functions that can fail should return a Result.

ü¶Ä Future‚Äôs always return the Poll enum but Future::Output will regularly be a Result.

ü¶Ä Let‚Äôs create a Future that could fail.

ü¶Ä This future will Return the time since the Linux Epoch, in seconds‚Ä¶

ü¶Ä ‚Ä¶but only if that time is divisible by two, otherwise we‚Äôll return an error.

ü¶Ä Then we‚Äôll call that function, from another async function, and here is the magic!

ü¶Ä .await gets the Poll::Ready value, which is a Result, and the question mark operator either unwraps the Ok variant or bubbles the Error variant.

ü¶Ä We‚Äôll handle our error outside our async code.

ü¶Ä A successful run looks like this‚Ä¶ and an unsuccessful one looks like this!

async 6

This doesn‚Äôt need to be the end of the chain either!

Imagine a Future that returns Poll<Result<Vec<T>, E>>, you could .await?.into_iter().map(|t: T| /* do some work */)... etc.

This is another one of those Rust features that make the language really sublime to use!

async 7

ü¶Ä Anyway, because the .await postfix works on any future, and async code will resume from where it left off, we can .await several futures one after the other

ü¶Ä .await essentially pauses the execution of the code until the ThreadTimer future is ready, then continues on from that point.

ü¶Ä So this code is amazing, right? ‚Ä¶ Right?

ü¶Ä No! This code is bad, actually, but the reason may not be immediately obvious.

ü¶Ä Let‚Äôs time how long this takes using Instant.

ü¶Ä The total time taken to run the timers is 3 seconds, which makes sense right?

ü¶Ä We have a two-second timer and a one-second timer, combined, that‚Äôs three seconds‚Ä¶ but that‚Äôs the problem‚Ä¶

Join

join 1

The two timers in the previous examples are stand-ins for some arbitrary work and are completely independent of each other.

We should not be waiting for the first timer to complete before working on the second timer, that defeats the benefits of asynchronous programming.

join 2

What would be more useful is if we do both bits of ‚Äúwork‚Äù at the same time.

To do that, we need both Futures to be polled at the same time.

This is often called ‚Äújoining‚Äù.

join 3

ü¶Ä Here is a rough example of what a Join might look like.

ü¶Ä I‚Äôm not going to go too deep into this code, because most of the time you‚Äôll end up using someone else‚Äôs join code (heck, even the standard library has a proposed future join macro).

ü¶Ä If you really want to see all the code, it‚Äôs in the online IRISS book.

ü¶Ä To gloss over it a little bit, the CollapsableFuture is a wrapper Future that I made that takes any other Future and stores the output after it‚Äôs Ready so that it doesn‚Äôt matter how many times you call .poll()‚Ä¶ you‚Äôll see why in a moment.

ü¶Ä When we create a new Join we pass in two Futures of any type, then pass those to new CollapsableFutures.

ü¶Ä If you need to wait three futures, make one of those two another Join‚Ä¶ or use someone else‚Äôs better code.

join 4

ü¶Ä Let‚Äôs implement Future for Join.

ü¶Ä For a Join with the generic types F1 and F2, our Output will be a tuple of F1::Output and F2::Output.

ü¶Ä When you poll Join, it polls the inner Futures.

ü¶Ä It does this every time, which you aren‚Äôt supposed to do, which is why I made the CollapsableFuture to keep my Join code simple.

ü¶Ä Once both polls report Ready, then we extract the actual output from collapsable future and return Ready from the Join.

join 5

Ideally, you won‚Äôt be writing inefficient Join‚Äôs like this one yourself.

Most async crates provide their own version of join, such as Tokio‚Äôs join! macro, and Smol‚Äôs .zip() function.

Even the Rust standard library has std::future::join which they‚Äôre trying to stabilize.

Over in the Real World

real world 1

Rust doesn‚Äôt come with its own Executors, or much in the way of Future utilities (other than what we‚Äôve covered here).

This is because the implementation details can vary significantly depending on what you‚Äôre doing.

For example, while here we‚Äôve used Threads, Threads aren‚Äôt necessary, aren‚Äôt always available, and aren‚Äôt particularly efficient in some situations.

real world 2

If you‚Äôre working on an embedded microcontroller, for example, rather than parking and unparking threads, you might use interrupts and exceptions to wake futures.

If you‚Äôre running code inside an operating system, it might be more efficient to wait on callbacks from the OS to know when to wake a given future.

real world 3

Rather than writing your own executors, though, you‚Äôll find that other people have provided executors for common use cases such as those in the Tokio and Smol crates.

These crates also come with a lot of utility types, functions, and macros, including substantially more efficient and ergonomic ways to join futures.

They also usually have code for common futures such as working with the file system or network traffic asynchronously, either via libraries or optional crate features.

real world 4

Beyond this there are also executor agnostic libraries that provide futures for more specific needs like reqwest which provides HTTP specific futures, or SQLX, sequel x, squelex‚Ä¶

which provides connectivity to a variety of different database flavors.

real world 5

So, most of the time when you work with async Rust in the real world, you won‚Äôt need to write executors, and you won‚Äôt need to implement the Future trait yourself.

You‚Äôll use third party libraries for futures at the outer bounds of your software, join futures with third party utilities, and you‚Äôll glue it all together with futures created with async blocks and functions.

real world 6

ü¶Ä Returning to our rather silly example from the very beginning of this video, your async code is less likely to look like what we‚Äôve seen so far and much more likely to look like this:

ü¶Ä Isn‚Äôt it beautiful?!

ü¶Ä Sure it‚Äôs simplistic, but there‚Äôs not a Future trait or Pin in sight, and that‚Äôs genuinely what async Rust programming is like in the real world 99% of the time!

ü¶Ä So why did I just try to explain all of this?

ü¶Ä Because my hope is that, when you understand what‚Äôs happening under the hood, it will help you make the best use of async and avoid‚Ä¶

Common Gotchas

gotchas 1

Other than forgetting you can and should poll as many futures together as possible, see the earlier section on Joining futures, there are two fairly common gotcha‚Äôs in async code.

Luckily, they‚Äôre much easier to spot when you have a fair understanding of what‚Äôs going on underneath.

gotchas 2

The first is blocking.

We used threads in our examples, but you may not end up using a threaded executor, and even when you do, some executors allow multiple futures to run on a single thread.

This means using any blocking code could prevent a thread from continuing until it‚Äôs complete, which could impact the execution of some or all of your other futures.

gotchas 3

This is an easier mistake to make than you might think.

For example, reading a file or TCP stream using the standard library will block your thread until the action is completed.

gotchas 4

Worse still is Mutex which will block a thread until the lock becomes available.

If there are two futures on the same thread, and one has a MutexGuard when the other requests a lock, the second future will block the thread, preventing the first thread from dropping the MutexGuard.

This causes a bug called ‚ÄúDeadlock‚Äù.

gotchas 5

If you cleverly noticed that I used standard library Mutexes earlier, good catch!

Luckily, the specific way they‚Äôre being used is safe as the locks are guaranteed to be on different threads.

gotchas 6

Libraries like Tokio and Smol either come with (or provide separately) their own interpretations of typically blocking behaviors that use Futures instead.

gotchas 7

The second gotcha is with borrowing and ownership.

As we‚Äôve seen, there‚Äôs complexity around Futures that requires Pinning to be a thing.

This is because a Future might be moved by its executor, and if it is, self-referential data would point at old (or worse, freed) memory.

But it can get even more complex than that.

gotchas 8

Rusts ownership model means that the lifetime of a reference must be trackable to make sure it doesn‚Äôt outlive its owned data.

This gets a lot more challenging with futures as the compiler needs to track references through deferred work.

Thinking even more carefully about owned data, references, and the memory and CPU tradeoffs of cloning data can make async code harder to reason about.

gotchas 9

It‚Äôs worth remembering that, particularly for smaller projects, you don‚Äôt have to use async if it‚Äôs causing your code to be more complicated than it would otherwise be.

Summary

summary 1

Hopefully, after this video, you have a bit more of an understanding about what‚Äôs going on when you use async in Rust.

There are a variety of crates that help provide utilities for async each in their own way, so before you choose executor crates and Future supporting libraries, it‚Äôs worth reading crate documentation to understand the options available and the choices made.

summary 2

While there are many more language features available to you, I hope I‚Äôve left you in a strong place to continue your journey.

resources 1

If you got all the way through IRISS and didn‚Äôt look at the official book yet‚Ä¶ how and why? It‚Äôs amazing!

resources 3

The official book goes into far more detail than IRISS, and makes fewer pains to avoid non-idiomatic examples, or crates, so you can argue, gets to the point more directly.

Particularly if you‚Äôve gone through IRISS already, the official book will be a sinch to get through and give you a much broader view of the language.

resources 4

For the more hands on, I can‚Äôt recommend Rustlings enough.

It‚Äôs a collection of short exorcises that don‚Äôt just tell you how Rust works, but get you to write it.

You run it locally, and you can only pass a section when your code works!

It mirrors the official book pretty closely so working through both at the same time can really support your learning.

resources 5

Start building

building 1

The best way to learn Rust is to start building.

Rust is one of the few languages that work in just about any field.

So what do you want to build?

building 2

To give you some food for thought, you can build command line apps, cross-platform GUI apps, full stack web apps, embedded microcontroller apps, machine learning tools, networking tools, video games, and even libraries that can be consumed by other programs and programming languages.

The list is essentially endless.

building 3

Early examples for me were;

building 4

A CSV to Json converter using SIR DAY‚Ä¶ SEER DE‚Ä¶ SIRD?

Serde, of no agreed pronunciation, is short for serialize, deserialize.

It‚Äôs the goto tool for converting string data formatted in a variety of file formats, into data in your application such as structs, and back again.

It‚Äôs also what lets me store data from my Job Tracking app as JSON files.

building 3

Another easier tool I built, this time for work, was a web API for reading specific details from a WordPress database using [Actix Web] and [Diesel].

It was 4 times faster and 5 times more memory efficient than a similar tool we‚Äôd build in TypeScript.

Actix Web is an incredibly fast web server framework that‚Äôs surprisingly easy to work with.

Diesel is a Database ORM that not only lets you read and write to databases, but can also manage things like table structure and migrations.

building 3

Something more fun was a two hundred and fifty thousand cell game of life in [Web Assembly] that ran at 60fps.

Web Assembly isn‚Äôt a framework or library like other tools I‚Äôm listing here, it‚Äôs a compile target.

You can compile Rust into Web Assembly.

I would even go so far as to argue that, thanks to how Rust works as a language, and especially the supporting tooling, Rust should be everyone‚Äôs first choice when writing high compute performance code for frontend web.

building 3b

But, to clarify, I don‚Äôt want to yuck anyones yum, there‚Äôs no single right choice for your programming language of choice and there are many factors to consider including personal preference.

WebAssembly 3 now comes with features that make it easier to transpile from other languages.

Rust still offers a good performance to effort ratio in my opinion but, you do you.

building 3

While learning Japanese, I wrote a command line flash card database using [Clap] and [Sqlx] (SQLX also of no concrete pronounciation).

Clap is a tool for parsing command line arguments as well as providing a common experience across cli applications.

Sqlx is another framework for working with Databases, it supports async out of the box, but doesn‚Äôt have the ORM or migration features built into Diesel.

building 4

My advice is to think of something you‚Äôre missing in your life, ideally something that‚Äôs limited in scope and achievable.

Plan out how you‚Äôd structure the solution, and try building it.

NewType

What is a type?

type 1

Why do we use types?

Forget newtypes, for now, why do we use types at all?

If I show you this binary value, can you tell me what it is?

type 2

We can rule out a few things.

It‚Äôs 64 bits, which means it‚Äôs definitely not a boolean or a character.

It‚Äôs also too long for an IPv4 address, and too short for an IPv6 address.

More likely it‚Äôs a number, either an integer or a float.

type 3

show program 00_what_is_this

Let‚Äôs try parsing the data as a few things.

Here‚Äôs the data.

For reference, this is it as bytes.

So it could be a huge integer

It‚Äôs probably not a float though.

Ah, well, this looks right

So it‚Äôs probably a short, UTF-8 encoded string advertising our new collection of videos, but, who‚Äôs to say really?

Without knowing for sure, we‚Äôre just guessing.

type 4

Types turn data into information.

Without knowing whether we‚Äôre looking at a number or a sequence of ascii characters, we‚Äôd have a really hard time writing code and, more importantly, what we write would be very error-prone.

Without type information, there‚Äôs nothing stopping us from accidentally passing a boolean to a function that expects a complex user structure.

type 5

We start having to depend constantly on runtime checks to make sure any data our functions receive is valid before trying to process it.

All modern languages (even ones we may not usually think of being ‚Äútyped‚Äù) come with their own built-in types that usually cover, at the very least, floats, strings, lists and objects (or dictionaries).

This helps us reason about the data stored in memory.

type 6

You‚Äôll notice I didn‚Äôt include integers in the bare minimum types, this is because some languages opt to store all numbers as double precision floating points.

This only really becomes an accuracy issue for integers over 2 to the power of 53 though, which is over 9 million billion, so I think we can give them a break.

type 7

But is it enough to consider "hello@example.com" to be a string?

It technically is a string, sure, and we could manipulate it as one, but‚Ä¶

type 8

In the context of our software, it might be that there is a meaningful difference between "hello@example.com" and "Hello, example.com", in the same way as there is a meaningful difference between "spaceFTW" and

  • six quintillion
  • two hundred and ninty two quadrillion
  • seven hundred and thirty one trillion
  • nine hundred and eighty billion
  • six hundred and sixteen million
  • three hundred and ninty six thousand
  • nine hundred and fifteen

Why normal types aren‚Äôt enough

show code 01_dates

problem 1

Here‚Äôs some code that represents years, months and days.

For simplicity, I‚Äôve use u64s for everything.

problem 2

Yes, we should probably use u8s for the month and day, but the size is irrelevant to the point, not every language has differently sized integers‚Ä¶ or as we discussed, integers at all‚Ä¶ and u64s will help me demonstrate a point at the end of the video.

problem 3

However, we can immediately identify some problems with this.

First, there‚Äôs nothing stopping us from using valid u64s to represent invalid data.

I can make all of these numbers invalid by changing them to zero

problem 4

Second, there‚Äôs nothing stopping us passing any valid number to a function that‚Äôs supposed to take a month.

show code 02_get_english_month_name

In this example we‚Äôve got a function that takes a month and returns the name of the month in English.

However, because you might pass in a u64 that‚Äôs not a valid month, that means we need to validate the parameter and return a Result.

problem 5

I‚Äôm using a unit struct for an error here, ideally, we‚Äôd go through the steps to impl Error, but I hope you‚Äôll forgive me if I skip that for this example.

problem 6

Our function checks that the number given to it is between one and twelve.

Unfortunately, it doesn‚Äôt stop us passing data to the function that isn‚Äôt supposed to be a month.

run code

Rust is happy to let us pass in data that‚Äôs supposed to represent a year or day, and that‚Äôs a mistake that may be missed even at runtime

problem 7

What I‚Äôd like you to take away from this is that concepts of Days, Months and Years are meaningfully different within the domain of our application here.

There is contextual information that we‚Äôre ignoring about the data.

We need to know more about the ‚Äútype‚Äù of data we‚Äôre dealing with beyond it just being a number!

This is what the newtype pattern is for.

Introducing newtype

newtype 1

To oversimplify a touch, Newtypes, are wrappers around existing types that provide the context for the information stored within.

First, let‚Äôs prevent days being passed into functions that take months.

newtype 2

show code 03_basic_newtype

Here, I‚Äôve wrapped our u64s in tuple structs, one for each of Year, Month and Day.

I‚Äôve also modified our function to explicitly take the Month type.

newtype 4

This is immediately more informative to anyone reading your code.

Even better, if we try to pass a Year or Day into the function, we get a compile time error that very clearly tells us what‚Äôs wrong.

newtype 5

Our second issue is that we can still produce invalid values such as Month(13).

We can fix this by controlling access to the inner data.

For example, we could restrict the instantiation of our types to a constructor that validates the input.

newtype 6

Let‚Äôs focus on Month.

show code 04_month_newtype

We need to make sure the interior of the struct can only be instantiated or edited by things we control.

In this code I‚Äôve moved the Month newtype into a month module

newtype 7

This lets us protect the internal data so only things inside the module can see or change it.

And we can control exposure through marking things explicitly as public.

So the interior of our Unit struct is not public, but our constructor is.

newtype 8

Now we can validate the month when it‚Äôs constructed, returning the error if necessary.

Since we can no longer instantiate an invalid month, we can fairly confidently remove the result from any functions like get_english_month_name.

We still technically have to deal with every possible u64 in the match statement, but theoretically it has to be between 1 and 12, and if it‚Äôs not, something has gone badly wrong so I think a panic is now in order.

newtype 9

I‚Äôd say there‚Äôs still one improvement we can make here, at least for months.

Our program is written in English, so why use numbers to represent the months in our code at all.

Plu, dealing with numbers are why we still need to have that panic in our get_english_month_name function.

newtype 10

show code 05_enum_newtype

We can change how we model Month in our code without changing its underlying numeric representation by changing it to an enum and specifying the enums discriminant type with the repr attribute.

newtype 11

Depending on your data you obviously may not need to specify this, and again, a u8 would be more appropriate here, but I still need to make that point later. :)

Anyway, by using an enum, we can remove the code branch that was theoretically impossible anyway AND I‚Äôd say this is a lot easier for anyone reading th code to understand.

newtype 12

So, that big point I‚Äôve been working up to‚Ä¶ these types only exist at compile time.

In memory, in our running program, these types are all exactly the same:

newtype 13

show code 06_newtype_size

In this program we construct each of the Month types we‚Äôve discussed (I‚Äôve had to give them different names here but don‚Äôt worry they‚Äôre otherwise identical)

We‚Äôve got our u64, our tuple struct, and our enum versions.

Each version looks very different but conceptualy represent the same information.

I found this function that can extract the memory of any given value regardless of type.

Passing each version of our month in for September gives us back a byte array.

Exactly the same byte array in fact.

This is why I stuck with u64s for what its worth.

newtype 14

Types aren‚Äôt real.

They provide us, the software engineers, information we need to structure our programs better and we can use the type system to prevent us from making mistakes.

But at runtime, it all dissapears

Tradeoffs?

tradeoffs 1

There seems to be a lot of extra work going on here.

We need to add more validation, extra error types (and all the extra work they‚Äôre supposed to involve that we skipped here), not to mention how verbose the match statements were for the enum version of our month newtype.

That‚Äôs true.

tradeoffs 2

But we‚Äôve been focused on the newtype, not the impact that it has across your program.

By moving our validation code into a single domain type, we‚Äôre decluttering the rest of our program.

tradeoffs 3

show code 07_newtype_size

Let‚Äôs think about a more complex type, like an email.

Using built-in types, we just create a validator and call it done:

tradeoffs 4

This code is straightforward, terse and requires little testing.

However, everytime we want to use a string as an email, we will need to run the validator.

This not only could risk the same email needing to be validated multiple times, but adds some significant risk, particularly as our code evolves.

Any time we don‚Äôt use the validator for a function that accepts an email string because we perhaps initially create it only in a context where the string has already been validated, we risk that function being reused with no validation somewhere else.

tradeoffs 5

Worse, it‚Äôs easy to imagine that our software might end up with multiple validation functions, each following slightly different rules.

In this example we‚Äôre using my ‚Äúgood enough, no false negatives‚Äù approach, but another engineer might write a different validator

Perhaps they come up with a regex which catches more bad emails but has a small chance of flagging a false negative.

Then we have two models of what an email should be inside our software, and they no longer agree with each other.

tradeoffs 6

show code 08_email_newtype

Here‚Äôs what it might look like to make an email newtype.

We‚Äôve added an Error type for potentially invalid addresses and, this time I did those extra steps to implement the Error trait.

tradeoffs 6b

Next we‚Äôve got our a constructor which calls to our validator, though the validator logic is identical.

tradeoffs 6c

We‚Äôve added one new test for the constructor, and the one for our validator is the same.

tradeoffs 7

Now, though, we only ever need to validate the email when we create the data type, which will usually be when we‚Äôre getting that data from an external source, for example, from a user or importing it from a database.

This will also mean we only have to look in one place if we need to change our Email logic, or fix any bugs.

Conclusion

conclusion 1

For a small amount of extra work, newtypes give us:

  • Centralised validation and error handling
  • Reduced complexity
  • More information to understand the domain we‚Äôre working in
  • And more defensive, safer to use code

conclusion 2
