Typestate

The typestate pattern encodes state into type information.

This is useful because, as we showed last time, this information exists when you write the program but not when you run it!

This lets us enforce domain logic, that is, how we map our code to real world problems, with minimal overhead.

State

state 1

slide 1

Let‚Äôs imagine a simplified pull request process.

Once a pull request ^ is open, it can ^ be approved ^ or rejected.

Multiple people ^ can approve it, but if anyone rejects it ^ (even after its approved) it can no longer be merged.

So long as the pull request is in the approved state, then ^ it can be merged.

state 2

Let‚Äôs try to model this in a ‚Äútraditional‚Äù cough bad cough way.

slide 2

First we‚Äôll create a status enum that can be Open, Approved, Rejected or Merged

click

For the purpose of our example the PullRequest type will have status, but we‚Äôll ignore any other data you might want to put on there for now.

state 3

slide 3

Our PR struct has three methods, approve, reject and merge.

state 4

slide 4

A PR can be approved if the status is Open or Approved.

If the status was not one of those, then we‚Äôll need to return an error to say that the status could not be changed.

slide 5

Similarly, our reject method will work if the status is open, or approved, but not if the PR has been merged.

In this case I‚Äôve also allowed you to Reject a Rejected PR because it didn‚Äôt make sense to me for that to be an error even though it‚Äôs not part of our process.

slide 6

Finally, our merge method only works if the PR is in the approved status, otherwise it will return an error.

state 5

We‚Äôve written up our three state change operations, but we‚Äôve had to write a lot of branching logic into each method to check that the operation is only being run on a PR in a valid state.

slide 8

To show how complex this gets, I‚Äôm listing here the tests for every valid state change to make sure they are successful, and, ^ the tests for every invalid state change to make sure they are not.

Typestate

camera, transition to slide 8

typestate 1

But what if instead of a PullRequest with a status, we represent that status in the Pull Requests type.

slide 9

In this case we have a separate pull request struct for each state the Pull Request can be in; Open, Approved, Rejected, Merged.

typestate 2

Now we can implement each struct separately.

slide 10

In PullRequestOpen, we can add the ‚Äúapprove‚Äù and ‚Äúreject‚Äù methods and there‚Äôs no need for branching or errors because we already know the PullRequest is in a valid state.

slide 11

Similarly, we can implement the PullRequestApproved methods and again, there‚Äôs no need for branching given our simple logic.

typestate 3

What‚Äôs really cool about this is that we don‚Äôt need to check the state and return a Result because it‚Äôs simply not possible to make an invalid change.

The merge method, for example, only exists on PullRequestApproved.

You can‚Äôt merge a rejected pull request because PullRequestRejected doesn‚Äôt have the necessary method.

typestate 4

camera

This also means we don‚Äôt need to write tests for invalid state changes because those methods do not exist on invalid types.

Advanced Typestate

advanced 1

One downside to this method of doing typestate is that if our Pull Request structs have lots of data associated with them (data we‚Äôve conveniently been skipping over for these examples), you have to repeatedly define that data for every type.

Heaven forbid you want to change something like the author value from a String to an Author newtype, you‚Äôll have to change it everywhere.

advanced 2

Luckily, we can improve this pattern with generics.

slide 12

First, we‚Äôll create a trait for our state, this is just to restrict our Pull Request generic later.

slide 13

We‚Äôll use unit structs to represent our states, ^ and implement the trait for each of them.

slide 15

We‚Äôre going back to having a single type for our Pull Request on which we can keep all the relevant data, but it‚Äôs a generic.

The generic type S is restricted to PullRequestState.

advanced 3

Now, importantly, when we create a generic type, we need to use the generic parameter somewhere inside it.

advanced 5

What‚Äôs cool though is that we could, if we needed to, attach state specific fields to the state structs instead of to the pull request.

slide 16

Moving on, I‚Äôve created a constructor to open a new PullRequest, and so long as this is the only method called Open, ^ there is no ambiguity, so we don‚Äôt need to specify the generic to use it.

advanced 6

We can now implement our state specific state changes on only the PullRequests that are currently in the right state.

slide 17

For the Open variant of our PullRequest we can approve or reject them.

As with the previous version there is no branching, we already know we‚Äôre in the correct state so we can move our data and return the new type.

Now you might be thinking, can‚Äôt we just spread the old PullRequest into the new one, they‚Äôre both PullRequest types?

Unfortunately, the generic means that the concrete types are different and Rust won‚Äôt allow us to just spread one into the other.

advanced 7

One way to mitigate the extra work this might create is to group properties into subtypes that could be moved more easily.

slide 18

Our Approved variant can be approved by more people, be rejected, or be merged.

As with our previous implementation of the pattern, Rejected and Merged pull requests can not have their status changed so we don‚Äôt need to add any methods to these types.

Conclusion

conclusion 1

The obvious downside to the pattern, at least in Rust, is having to copy each property across manually.

However, you only have to write these methods once, but the benefits just keep giving.

Utilising the typestate pattern requires writing a lot more code initially but that code has fewer branches, fewer (or in this case no) potential error states, and everything is logically subdivided making maintaining the code more trivial.

It‚Äôs almost always going to be better to take a small upfront cost for cheaper easier to use code in the long run.

conclusion 2

Fluent Interface

intro 1

We often group data together into structs or objects.

slide 1

For example, here‚Äôs a simple User struct that has a username, an email and a date of birth.

intro 2

Our User requires a Username, so we have a constructor that takes that, but email and date of birth are optional and can be set later.

slide 2

If we wanted to add them later, that code would look like this.

This immediately adds some complexity.

intro 3

Depending on the nature of the data we might want to manage access through getters and setters, particularly if we need to do any validation, or manipulation, etc.

slide 3

In our User example, it might be good to not have to specify the Option when setting email and date of birth, which can be done inside a setter.

slide 4

This is a bit cleaner, and we now have a good place to put validation if we need it too.

intro 4

However, this is still a little bit clunky as we have to refer to the underlying variable (yuki) multiple times.

We can make this more ‚Äúfluent‚Äù.

slide 5

In this version our setters are basically identical, but now we‚Äôre returning a reference to the object being mutated.

slide 6

This allows us to chain the setter methods, making the code cleaner and easier to read.

Rust Quirks

camera

quirks 1

There are two quirks to this pattern that impact Rust specifically.

Firstly, this pattern works in many languages, and particularly in languages that use things like pass by reference and copy on write by default, you can sort of stop thinking about it there.

Rust has very specific memory management rules to think about though.

This means we need to consider whether our fluent interface will use references or take and return ownership.

quirks 2

There are pros and cons to each approach.

slide 6 still

Going back to our previous example, we need to make sure the owned data exists somewhere before we start modifying it, and that the data is mutable.

That‚Äôs why we stored yuki first, then modified it, effectively two steps.

slide 7

Instead of taking references lets try taking ownership and returning a new version of the object with the data we want to add.

quirks 3

Soooo‚Ä¶ I‚Äôve changed the prefix from ‚Äúset‚Äù to ‚Äúwith‚Äù because this better reflects the ‚Äútake ownership and return something new ‚Äòwith‚Äô the extra data‚Äù but I don‚Äôt know if there‚Äôs an official idiom or pattern like this, so I‚Äôm open to better suggestions.

quirks 4

Anyway, by passing ownership back and forth instead, we can now move both creation and adding additional data all into a single chain:

slide 8

In addition to this giving us a slightly nicer flow, you might notice that yuki doesn‚Äôt need to be mutable, we‚Äôre encapsulating the mutability inside the methods where we need it.

slide 9

However, there‚Äôs a downside to this too. If we did want to modify a single value, we‚Äôd have to make sure we take ownership back from the method.

That said, because we‚Äôre using the with prefix, you could use both setters and‚Ä¶ uh ‚Äúwithers‚Äù.

quirks 5

So that‚Äôs the first quirk.

The other quirk is more of a nice touch.

What if our setter could fail?

slide 10

Obviously, it‚Äôs Rust, we need to return a Result.

quirks 6

In this case we‚Äôll check if a user is older than 21‚Ä¶

No, this doesn‚Äôt really make sense at this point, let‚Äôs not worry too much about the domain logic for our demo app though

slide 11

Luckily, the question mark operator allows us to unwrap an Ok, which means we can continue the chain so long as we‚Äôre able to bubble the error.

Conclusion

conclusion 1

In conclusion, using the FluentAPI pattern allows us to write much more readable code!

Not only do setters let us encapsulate validation as well as mundane things like wrapping our types in Options, but method chaining reduces code clutter.

Rust requires slightly more thought than some other languages but not having to think about Copy On Write is nice too.

conclusion 2

Builder

Intro

The builder pattern allows us to construct complex types in steps.

For example, in our previous video, you don‚Äôt have to have watched it, we had a ‚ÄòUser‚Äô struct that had a username, an email and a date of birth.

In that video, we allowed the date of birth to be set later, but a user also had to be older than 21 (which I mentioned was silly to do after the user was created).

Intro 2

If we were to make all of these fields required, creating the struct gets a bit unwieldy.

Do we instantiate it manually or write a constructor?

If we manually instantiate it, we can‚Äôt validate the fields.

If we write a single constructor, the parameter names are slightly obfuscated, but worse than that, we have to cram all our validation into a single function.

Intro 3

This isn‚Äôt so bad in our example User struct‚Ä¶

but what if you‚Äôre working with a more complicated structure with numerous fields many of which require validation?

This is where the Builder Pattern comes in.

Intro 4

Instead of constructing the object directly, we use another type to manage the construction.

This Builder type collects data about the struct we want to create, then has a finalizer that creates the struct from the data we‚Äôve given it.

There are arguably two versions of this pattern: a simpler version that will work in just about any language that I‚Äôm going to refer to as ‚ÄúBuilder Lite‚Äù, and a more complex version that only works in languages with strict generic typing, the ‚ÄúTypestate Builder‚Äù.

Each has their own pros and cons, so lets go over them.

Builder Lite

The traditional builder pattern uses a type that mirrors the type you want to build but everything is optional.

We use methods to update each field, and then have a finalizer that takes the data we‚Äôve stored in the builder and attempts to convert it into the target type.

Let‚Äôs build one for our User example.

Before we start on the Builder we need to set up an Error type as there are a lot of ways this could go wrong.

The UserBuilder itself looks just like the User, except the fields are all optional.

Each of our ‚Äúwithers‚Äù will set the relevant value and return a new version of the UserBuilder with the new value.

lite 2

Am I trying to make ‚Äúwithers‚Äù a thing? Yes.

Will I succeed? No, not a chance.

lite 3

Our first potential error is when setting the date of birth, where we test if the user is at least 21.

This doesn‚Äôt matter to our Fluent Interface in Rust so long as you‚Äôre using the Builder in a context where you can bubble the Error.

Our finalizer method is called ‚Äúbuild‚Äù and simply checks to see if all the Optional parameters have been set.

If we missed any values we‚Äôll return an appropriate error or if everything is ok, we‚Äôll return the built User type.

Here‚Äôs an example of where the User will be successfully built.

lite 4

Here‚Äôs an example where we‚Äôll get an error.

The biggest pro of this pattern is its really easy to understand at a glance and pretty easy to maintain.

The biggest con though is that we should know, at compile time, that that second builder was never going to succeed.

Typestate Builder

typestate 1

Let‚Äôs use the TypeState pattern to make that problem go away.

If you‚Äôre not already familiar with this pattern we have a video on it, but the TL;DW is that we can bake the state of something into its Type, and then decide what your allowed to do with data based on its Type.

Let‚Äôs make a TypeState Builder for our User.

First we can edit our Error down to a single event now, the others won‚Äôt be possible when we‚Äôre done.

Next, we‚Äôre going to use generic for each of the properties in our builder.

typestate 2

These generics can either be the type they‚Äôre supposed to be, or something that represents the fact we haven‚Äôt set them yet.

To achieve this, I‚Äôve created an ‚ÄúUnset‚Äù unit struct, and a trait to restrict each generic (this may not be necessary, but it is tidy).

We‚Äôll implement each trait for Unset, and for the type we actually want to use there.

Now we can create our Builder and the properties are all generics restricted by the relevant traits

typestate 3

So U can be Username or Unset, E can be EmailAddress or Unset and D can be DateOfBirth or Unset

We‚Äôre going to implement UserBuilder three times, each with different generic variants.

The first one we‚Äôll implement is when all of our generics are Unset, this allows us to have a constructor for the Builder that returns a version where everything starts off Unset.

Next we want to implement the UserBuilder for all generics where we don‚Äôt know what the generic type is.

typestate 4

Each method here consumes the Builder and returns a new Builder with the data we wanted set.

This updates the type, setting the generic we care about to that particular type but keeping the others as whatever type they were when the method was called.

For example, the ‚Äúwith_username‚Äù method will return a UserBuilder with the first generic parameter set to Username, but the other two will just be whatever they were when that method was called.

typestate 4

Same for the ‚Äúwith_email‚Äù method.

The ‚Äúwith_date_of_birth‚Äù method is a touch different because it returns a Result, but the Ok variant will still inherit whatever concrete types U and E were set to when the method was called.

Finally, we‚Äôll implement UserBuilder where all of our generics are now the types we want them to be.

typestate 5

Here‚Äôs where we put our finaliser method.

Because this method is only implemented for the concrete version of UserBuilder where all of our generics match the types in our User struct, we can just copy them straight over.

When we come to use this pattern, we need to provide all the necessary data or the user can not be built.

typestate 6

The build method simply doesn‚Äôt exist unless we‚Äôve done that, so this code won‚Äôt compile, specifically telling us that build does not exist for UserBuilder<Username, Unset, Unset>.

This is objectively‚Ä¶ awesome.

There are two big pros to this pattern, the obvious one is that we can not muck up building our user type, but its also worth pointing out that we‚Äôve removed all but one branch in our code, which will provide a significant runtime benefit.

The downside though is this code is just more complex to look at, particularly for newer engineers where all those triangle brackets might be intimidating.

Conclusion

conclusion 1

Should you just use the more complex but safer and faster TypeState Builder?

That probably depends on your team and your project requirements, but my advice is to trust yourself and your team.

It might require talking through a couple of times, especially if you‚Äôre building something particularly complex with your Builder, but getting out of our comfort zones helps us develop our skills.

conclusion 2

If you enjoyed this video, don‚Äôt forget to like and subscribe.

If you really liked the video, you can become a member of the channel or join the Patreon, see the description for more.

Monads

intro 1

A quick intro, I‚Äôm going to explain Monads, but don‚Äôt panic.

After this sentence we won‚Äôt use the words monads, monodic, monoid, functor, endofunctor or even category.

intro 2

That‚Äôs not because these words aren‚Äôt important, or learning about them isn‚Äôt interesting and enriching‚Ä¶

‚Ä¶ it‚Äôs because you don‚Äôt need to know these words to understand M‚Ä¶ this pattern!

I think the terminology can be off-putting which is a deep shame because this pattern is mwah chefs kiss.

intro 3

When programming, we regularly take a value and perform some operation to get a new value.

In fact its pretty much all we do, right?

intro 4

Let‚Äôs say we have the value 5 which is an integer.

We could square the value which gives us 25 which is still an integer‚Ä¶

or we could turn the value into a string which is still "5" but is now a different type.

intro 5

If we turn these operations into function like this‚Ä¶

we can think about these functions as taking something of type T and returning something of type U (even if T and U are the same type).

intro 6

I‚Äôm going to briefly swap to using TypeScript to explain this next bit because Rust simply won‚Äôt let me write code this way, but let‚Äôs say we have a function that returns either a number or nothing represented by ‚Äúnull‚Äù.

The function isn‚Äôt important, it just represents that some functions, by their nature, may need to return different types depending on what happened when they were called.

intro 7

But our functions (reproduced in typescript here) only take numbers.

This means we can‚Äôt just pass a variable that might be null into these functions.

intro 8

We need to check if the value is null and only pass it in if it‚Äôs not (this is a process called ‚Äúnarrowing‚Äù).

We can do this with branching, typescript is smart enough to know that the ‚Äúif‚Äù makes it impossible for the value to be null in this branch so this works‚Ä¶

but it obviously reads terribly, and adds a lot of boilerplate if we have to do this a lot.

intro 9

The next logical step is, if we‚Äôre just checking for null, we could abstract this to a generic function, which in typescript would look like this.

Here we‚Äôre passing our function that takes T and returns U into another function that takes T or null, and returns U or null.

If the input isn‚Äôt null, we run the passed function, otherwise we return null.

Now we can reuse this code a lot more easily but there‚Äôs still a lot of to-ing and fro-ing.

Also, none of this works in Rust.

intro 10

There‚Äôs another logical step we can take though, one that also lets us get back to Rust.

One that‚Äôs a little more ‚Äúfluent‚Äù if you will.

Mmmmmmo‚Ä¶. Containers

containers 1

At the moment we‚Äôre dealing with a value that could be type T, or it could be the concept of nothing.

There are two separate types.

containers 2

What if we wrapped these types in a container that could hold either T or nothing.

We‚Äôll call our container M for Mmmm reasons.

containers 3

So we have our container, M<T or nothing>, and our functions f(T) returning U

We can give our container a way to take that function and return a new container, M<U or nothing>.

containers 4

Rust actually has a built-in type that already does this, but to explain the pattern we‚Äôre going to reproduce it.

We‚Äôll start by creating an Enum called Maybe because maybe it contains a value, or maybe it contains nothing.

containers 5

We‚Äôll then give it a method that will map from Maybe<T> to Maybe<U> using the provided function, f(T) -> U.

This function is functionally identical to our if_not_null function we wrote in TypeScript but operates directly on an owned version of the Maybe.

Now when we call our Rust equivalent function to get even time, we‚Äôll get a Maybe<i64> back.

containers 6

We can map the Maybe<i64> using square.

Because square takes an i64 and returns an i64, using it on Maybe<i64> returns another Maybe<i64>.

When we use to_string which takes an i64 and returns a String, our Maybe<i64> turns into a Maybe<String>.

Finally, we can print this to the screen by examining the Maybe directly.

containers 7

That‚Äôs all well and good, but it seems like this hasn‚Äôt saved us much over what we were doing with if_not_null in TypeScript.

Where this pattern shines is that in TypeScript we were dealing with a value that could have two types.

By encapsulating it in a Mmmmm‚Ä¶ Container, we‚Äôre only dealing with one type, which means we‚Äôve built ourselves a fluent interface for managing our data.

containers 8

This means we can inline all of our maps, and it becomes far more readable than if we‚Äôd inlined our function calls.

For what its worth, this pattern works perfectly fine in TypeScript too.

Variations

variations 1

By now, you‚Äôve probably worked out that our Maybe type is us creating a less fantastic Rust Option.

Our containers can be more flexible than just T or nothing though.

It‚Äôs perfectly reasonable for your container to potentially hold multiple things.

variations 2

Let‚Äôs say we have a container that could hold types T or V.

This container should not only be able to take f(T) -> U which will give us a container potentially holding U or V, but it should also be able to take f(V) -> W which will give us a container potentially holding T or W.

A good example of this in Rust is the Result enum which holds an Ok variant of T and an Error variant of E

variations 3

However, we can‚Äôt just hand over f(T) -> U or f(V) -> W and hope for the best because what if both variants hold the same type.

It‚Äôs not unheard of for both types to be either a unit type or a string, I mean, it‚Äôs not ideal, but it‚Äôs not impossible.

Rust‚Äôs result type has separate methods for changing the Ok variant or the Error variant, the former still being ‚Äúmap‚Äù and the later being ‚Äúmap_err‚Äù.

Rules / Guidelines

rules 1

I‚Äôm sure I‚Äôll have made some hackles rise in my description of this pattern, so I do want to explain there are some specific rules that make this pattern what it is, it‚Äôs not just any old container.

Firstly, for a given container, a value representable as a type inside that container, should be convertable to that container without changing its value.

In our Maybe example (as well as in Option and Result), we simply pass the value to one of the enum variants, so the inner value doesn‚Äôt change.

rules 2

Second, there needs to be a way to apply a function to the inner types such that if you apply f(T) -> U to a container potentially holding a value of T then you get a container potentially holding a value of U.

In our Maybe, applying f(T) -> U to Maybe<T> returns Maybe<U> regardless of whether it contained a value of T or not.

rules 3

In some languages the rules are a little stricter and the pattern works very slightly differently, but there are plenty of videos that can explain this in more detail.

I just wanted to explain the basic pattern in an easy to digest way because I think it‚Äôs an extremely cool pattern, one that‚Äôs surprisingly crucial to how Rust works.

Summary

summary 1

This pattern can significantly reduce complexity, removing a lot of cognitive overhead when reading and writing code.

Rust is filled with this pattern, and in fact wouldn‚Äôt work as a language without it, we just don‚Äôt use that name because it feels overly complex.

I hope I‚Äôve aptly explained the pattern without all the scary words, but if you do want to learn more about this pattern warts and all, there‚Äôs a lot of great YouTube videos out there, I‚Äôll recommend some in the description of this one.

summary 2

If you enjoyed this video, don‚Äôt forget to like and subscribe.

If you really liked the video, you can become a member of the channel or join the Patreon, see the description for more.

Next time we‚Äôre going to talk about dependency injection, which is crucial to building complex yet maintainable software.

I hope I‚Äôll see you then.

Lifetimes in 60s

Rust lifetimes in 60 seconds

In Rust, all data is owned by something, let‚Äôs pretend it‚Äôs a crab.

When the crab goes out of scope, that data ceases to exist.

You can pass the crab up and down the stack, but if we had to pass ownership like this all the time it‚Äôd get really messy.

Instead, we can create a reference to data, let‚Äôs pretend it‚Äôs a kite.

The crab still owns the data, but the kite allows access to it.

The kite can now go out of scope, but the crab still keeps the data.

Lifetimes let us trace where the reference goes, like the string that connects the crab to the kite.

The kite can fly up and down the stack, so long as it never flies below the ground (where the crab is standing).

A function can take any number of kites in and return any number of kites out.

Sometimes it‚Äôs obvious to us, which incoming kites are attached to which outgoing kites, we just have to tell the compiler which kite strings are which.

Sometimes we can‚Äôt determine that, in which case we tie kite strings together and the returned kites can only go as low as the highest crab on the hill.

Let‚Äôs Build: A Job Tracker - Part 5

What‚Äôs wrong with what we have:

  • StoreContext is in UI for some reason
  • Lots of very similar repeated code
  • Adding new types requires a lot of boilerplate (eg, Values aren‚Äôt really any different from Flags or Roles in the way they are stored and recalled)

Storable objects have specific traits to allow them to optionally be:

  • stored (needs an ID)
  • recalled by ID (needs an ID)
  • recalled by name (needs a name)
  • recalled by company ID (needs a company name)

This is going to be a lot of traits which need to be inscope, we should use a prelude

A store for a generic Storable can use the traits to apply logic. There‚Äôs no real difference between a flag and a role in terms of how its stored and recalled, therefore there should be no difference in logic.

So we can have something like:

  • trait StoreById<T> where T: GetId
  • trait RecallById<T> where T: GetId
  • trait RecallByName<T> where T: GetName

We can create generic stores for storables that match certain traits, then build those generics together. Eg, a CompanyStore can be a trait that combines

  • StoreById<Company>
  • RecallById<Company>
  • RecallByName<Company>

If we have a store that already implements those traits, then we get it for free

A collection of stores should be able to determine which store an object belongs to by its type. <- This will be the harder part, may need phantom data in Store types ü§î

A collection of stores can implement the trait CompanyStore but we may be able to make it even more generic than that if we can go from generic type to property in stores

One way to do this might be to have a trait called HasStore<T> -> S and then implement:

  • HasStore<Company> -> impl CompanyStore
  • HasStore<Role> -> impl RoleStore
  • Etc

Suggested structure:

/storable
  /property
    mod.rs
    has_id.rs
    has_name.rs
    has_company.rs
  /object
    mod.rs
    company.rs
    role.rs
    flag.rs
    value.rs
/store
  /property
    mod.rs
    store.rs (probably both store and recall by id)
    recall_by_name.rs
    recall_by_company.rs
  /medium
    mod.rs
    stub.rs
    json.rs
  /object_store
    (probably collections of compound traits?)
  /stores
    mod.rs // HasStore<T> trait here?
    /mediums
      mod.rs

Notes

  • Check what impl does on parameters
